"THE BULLETIN" BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated (which is published price with
1 postage added), Books and Periodicals in the subjoined lists will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
“the BANJOES” BOOK.
The Bulletin is proud of its “ Banjo ” in
Angus and Robertson’s admirable dress —a
close imitation of Methuen’s original edition
of Kipling’s “ Barrack-Room Ballads.’
Hitherto, Australian authors have mostly
had to die before their typographical luck set
in : the new era is indeed welcome. And
as the publishers have Lawson and Daley in
hand for similar treatment, and other poets
and prosers will doubtless come along, it
looks as if a library of Australian authors,
printed in Australia, may some day be no
idle dream.
But since “Barrack-Room Ballads and
Other Verses” was taken as a typographical
model, it might possibly have been better to
take a hint from the title as well. ‘ ‘ Bush
Ballads and Other Verses ” sounds better
than “ The Man from Snowy River and
Other Verses,” and Gordon’s lien isn’t
absolute. “Gordon’s Poems” has swallowed
up “ Bush Ballads and Galloping Rhymes ”
for the public eye and the reader’s ear; and,
if a title should be descriptive, “Bush
Ballads” are the title for “The Banjo’s'
verses (“ A. B. Paterson ” sounds strange,
though one may get used to it in time).
The author has preferred to hint at his own
personality rather than at the character of
his verse ; and—it really doesn’t matter.
Yet it is always as a bush balladist that
one thinks of “ Banjo.” In “Rolf Boldre-
wood’s ” preface (The Bulletin doesn't con-
sider any Australian book improved by
“Rolf Boldrewood’s ” preface since Louis
Becke’s “Modern Buccaneer” was published
—“by Rolf Boldrewood ”), he says that the
collection “ comprises the best bush ballads
written since the death of Lindsay Gordon.”
The reference might be misleading to those
who do not know the books. Gordon,
indeed, called some of his verses “Bush
Ballads,” but (quite unheeding the matter of
form) there is hardly a hint of the ballad
spirit in any of them. If our true balladist
is no introspective pessimist ; and then Gor-
don had too much “culture,” too many of
the rhymester’s airs and graces, ever to be
natural—and a “ballad” which does not
spring spontaneously from the soul has no
right to the title.
The essence of the ballad is simplicity,
and simplicity is the last thing Gordon
dreamt of achieving. When he is natural,
it is because he has written too lazily or too
hurriedly to be artful; all his efforts are in
the direction of elaboration. Take the dedica-
tion to the “Bash Ballads” themselves—
perhaps the prettiest bit of verse Gordon
wrote. His songs, he said, came —
In the deep’ning of dawn, when it dapples
The dusk of the sky
With streaks like the reld’ning of apples
The ripening of rye ;
To eastward when, cluster by cluster.
Dim stars and dull planets that muster
Wax wan in a world of white lustre
That floods far and high.
What balladist ever compassed such al-
literative effects -or strove for them ? And
how much does alliteration bother “ The
Banjo ” 1
“The Banjo” is just the ordinary bush
bard sublimated —raised to the «th power,
as it were. He is not akin to the bush
“ poet,” for his work is not, and does not
profess to be, all poetry, though it is some-
times highly poetic, when (as in “Kiley’s
Run ”) the subject suggests pathos. But
“The Banjo’s” sense of pathos is no more
than any healthy male may possess without
feeling his appetite a bit the worse—no more
than any average bushman has when he
comes across a deserted selection, or
his girl of yore who married somebody
else. With such an invitation nine
bushmen out of ten will sit down, if they are
hy themselves, to compose doggerel which
they call “poetry.” But it isn’t poetry.
Poetry is generally more or less of an un-
healthy growth, a morbid secretion; and
doesn’t come naturally to the man who
thinks nothing of riding 70 or 80 miles, or
shearing seven or eight score sheep, every
day for a week together. That man doesn’t
write poetry ; his liver is too good ; but if he
is in the mood, and has a reading turn, he
may compose very decent verse.
“ The Banjo’s ” verse is more than decent :
it is good, and is strongly leavened with the
divine gift of humor. It isn’t very deep,
and the deeper it aims at being the less it
succeeds ; but it is stirring, manly stuff,
which rejoices one to read and remember.
Mr. Paterson, indeed, Jike the old Cavalier
lyrists, is able to combine the power to do
and the power to write. Wolfe could only
take Quebec, he couldn’t write Gray’s
“ Elegy ” ; but “ The Banjo ” rides and wins
his steeplechases, and then, one may believe,
sits down with a still tingling pulse and con-
veys the tingle to the pulses of his readers.
though his horse-poems, such as the
splendid “ Old Pardon,” may please most
readers, he is perhaps at his best when,
without too obvious labor, he touches the
humorous or sentimental side of some com-
monplace bush scene or incident, as in “The
Two Devines,” or the first part of “Kiley’s
Run,’’ or a dozen other pieces in his new
volume. He is at his worst in pieces like
“Lost’’and “Only a Jockey,” and one or
two others injudiciously admitted. He has
some verbal faults and a few lines of which
the “merits indeed are but slight ” (as he
says in the weak prelude whose place should
have been taken by “Daylight is Dying”).
But withal he is “The Banjo,” of whose
verse every Australian already knows at
least some snatches, and we love him.
The book, which, it is easy to predict,
will have a great and permanent sale, does
exceeding credit to the printers, Websdale,
Shoosraith and Cos., of Sydney.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Edward Jenks (once law-professor at Melb.
University) in his recently-published “His-
tory of the Australasian [Colonies ” (“ The
History” he calls it), only “believes”
“ payment of members ” is in force in Aus-
tralasia. He ignores the financial crisis of
’93 (probably because there are no handy
authorities like Blair and Rusdeu to dish
up) ; and, while mentioning a number of
nonentities as prominent politicians, he dis-
cards men like Service, Vogel, Parkes,
Dailey, and others. Jenks says there is no
originality in Australian politics, and on the
next page remarks that experience has
ripened originality. The Saturday Review,
which notes most of these things and some
others similar, characterises the book as
slovenly—to which English adjective, for
Australian purposes, the great Australian
adjective may advantageously be prefixed.
“The Pocket Guide to Cycling” (Is. 3d.),
though written for English readers, has
sufficient hints of all-round applicability to
make it useful to Australasian cyclists.
“I never had a spark of imagination,”
says the supposed writer of Conan Doyle’s
“Stark-Munro Letters” (35., 45.). Inten-
tionally or not, the line fits Doyle himself
truly. His new book is very solid and
Scottish, in the style of “ Round the Red
Lamp,” with hardly as much interest as that
set of stories, but with more than enough to
be well worth reading. It has become quite
clear, however, that Doyle will never make
literature ; his new book rises only to the
rank of careful journalism, with a good deal
of diary-transcription, a little pithy philo-
sophy, and some rhetorical effects labori-
ously worked up. But not an atom
of fancy. There is a suggestion of
“ Breakfast-Table ” methods in the aphor-
isms, impressions, and instances, and Doyle
should have credit for the remarks on dog-
matic religion—British readers being apt to
punish authors who interfere with their con-
ventional beliefs. Here is a good sample of
the clever and conscientious stuff with which
“ The Stark-Munro Letters.” are filled. A
smart doctor is initiating a tyro in his busi-
ness methods.
“There are one or two elementary rules to be
observed in the way of handling patients,” he re-
marked, seating himself on the table and swinging.
“ The most obvious is that you must never let
them see that you want them. It should be pure
condescension on your part seeing them at all;
and the more difficulties you throw in the way of
it, the more they think of you. Break your patients
in early, and keep them well to heel. Never make
the fatal mistake of being polite to them. Many
foolish young men fall into this habit, and are
ruined in consequence. Now, this is my form.”
He sprang to the door, and, putting his two hands
to his mouth, he bellowed: “Stop your con-
founded jabbering down there ! I might as well
be living above a poultry show ! There, you see,”
he added to me, “they will think ever so much
more of me for that.”
“ But don’t they get offended ? ” I asked.
“ I’m afraid not. I have a name for this sort
of thing now, and they have come to expect it.
But an offended patient—l mean a thoroughly
insulted one —is the lirest advertisement in the
world. If it is a woman, she runs clacking about
among her friends until your name becomes a
household word, and they all pretend to sympa-
thise with her, and agree among themselves that
you must be a remarkably discerning man. I
quarrelled with one man about the state of his
gall duct, and it ended by my throwing him down
the stairs. What was the result ? He talked so
much about it that the whole village from which
he came, sick and well, trooped to see me. The
little country practitioner who had been buttering
them lip for a quarter of a centu y found i bat he
might as well put up his shutters. It s human
nature, my boy, and you can’t alter it. Eh, what 2
Y ou make yourself cheap and you become cheap.
You put a high price on yourself and
they rate you at that price. Suppose I
set" up in Harley-street to-morrow, and
made it all nice and easy, with hours
from ten to three, do you thin . I should get a
patient 2 1 might starve first. How would I
work it 2 I should lot it be known, that I only
saw patients frdm midnight until two in the
morning, and that bald-headed people must pay
double. That would set people talking, their
curiosity would be stimulated, and in four months
the streets would be blocked all night. Eh, what ?
laddie, you’d go yourself. That’s my principle
here. I often come in of a morning and send
them all about their business, tell them I’m going
off to the country for a day. I turn away forty
pounds, and it’s worth four hundred as an adver-
tisement ! ”
NOTES.
Perhaps of all literary Australians H. B.
Marriott Watson has “ arrived ” furthest.
His recent appointment to the co-editorship
of the Saturday Review is a blue ribbon
the like of which no other of our prosers has
achieve ! at 30 ; and Mr. Watson’s fine talent
has not yet found its limits. lie is a native
of Melbourne ; and his father, the Rev. H. C.
Marriott Watson (now of St. John’s Church,
Christchurch, M.L.), is also Australian-born
of parents Australian-born, and a writer of
no mean repute.
When The Bulletin in a recent issue
labelled Alex. Montgomery “ Scottish,” it
did unintentionally “another injustice to
Ireland,” where (in the North) the Bornean
story-teller came to light in ’47. Yet his
family is that Franco-Scottish one which con-
tributed General Ralph Montgomery to the
cause of American independence; so in
essence, maybe, the label was deserved. In-
tended originally for the diplomatic service
(Mr. Montgomery alleges he had special dis-
qualifications for the diplomatic service), a
taste for adventure sent him surreptitiously
to salt-water. He was fascinated by
. . . the black wharves and the slips,
And the sea-tides tossing free ;
And Spanish sailors with bearded lips,
And the beauty and mystery of the ships,
And the magic of the sea.
Then came some rough experiences in the
North Atlantic trade, followed by a couple
of years spent in studying medicine ashore,
till the roving spirit sent him to the Medi-
terranean (where he was stabbed and run
down) and then to the East, where he
wandered in a scientific spirit over the
Malay Archipelago. Coming to Australia,
he added a few bush experiences to his pile,
and drifted into literature by way of his own
adventures.
Writes D.R.W. : The Bulletin some
time ago quoted a par. to the effect
that a brother of Lindsay Gordon,
with papers to prove his identity, had
died in a N.S.W. up-country hospital.
This, from a letter lately received from
Major-General F. T. Whingate, may have
interest: “As to Lindsay Gordon having
had a brother—it is absolute nonsense. I
knew the family intimately, and can state
positively that Gordon's parents had but
three children, viz. : A.L.G. and his two
sisters. What is more, I never at any time
heard mention made of any other children
who had formed part of the family, and who
had died or disappeared. Had there been
such, it is almost impossible that I should not
have been aware of the fact..
Gordon and Gotch are agents for “Beau-
tiful Britain,” a portfolio of photographs of
historical English buildings and sights, now
publishing at Bd. each number posted.
To Correspondents. — Ipswich : Haven’t
an American edition of Twain’s “Yankee,”
but a recollection of a hastily-scanned copy
gives no ground for saying the Eng. edition
differs. Anyhow, the Eng. edition is quite
enough to knock the stuffing out of a straw
monarch. Just let this soak in ;
You see my kind of loyalty was loyalty to one’s
country, not to its institutions or its office-holders.
The country is the real thing, the substantial
thing, the eternal thing; it is the thing to watch
over, and care for, and he loyal to; institutions
axe extraneous, they are its mere clothing, and
clothing can wear out, become ragged, cease to be
comfortable, cease to protect the body from winter,
disease, and death. To be loyal to rags, to shout
for rags, to worship rags, to die for rags —that is a
loyalty of unreason, it is pure animal; it belongs
to monarchy, was invented by monarchy; let
monarchy keep it.
W.Y. ; Heine’s poems are untranslatable,
and nearly all his fragrance is lost in Bow-
ring’s translation, which is, however, correct
in the main and cheap (45.). There are
many versions of scattered poems ; James
Thomson’s are the happiest we know.
Here is his rendering of the familiar “ Ein
Fichtenbaum steht einsam ” :
A pine-tree standeth lonely
In the North on an upland bare ;
It r.tandeth whitely shrouded
With snow, and sleepeth there :
It dreameth of a palm-tree,
Which far in the East alone
In mournful silence standeth
On its ridge of burning stone.
Heine’s prose works are splendidly done in
8 vols. (445.) by “ Hans Breitmann” Leland;
but you can get several good selections at
Is. 9d. each.

Medical.
IT MAT BE SELDOM
That Medical Advertisements can be relied on, bub
we here assert the following facts, which can be
borne out from Testimonials in our possession,
thousands may see this, and treat it as some
announcements deserve, but in this they would
make a great mistake,
AND FOR THIS REASON :
by taking notice of what we have already done
and can do, as proved by thousands of grateful
acknowledgments of our skill which we hold, it

A DISEASE, not a CRIME,
The constant abuse of alcohol fosters a crave—
a crave insatiable and deadly. It deprives valu-
able men of all reason and respect; it drives them
to acts of indecency and crime, and to drink what
their palates may even repel. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless, and
physical, as well as mental attention, is urgently
required.
The Orient Institute
has established a wonderful reputation in the
curing of this awful disease, and, during the past
18 months, many prominent men of our colony
have been restored to health, sobriety and
position. Alcoholic stimulants become not only
unnecessary, bub, in the majority of cases, un-
palatable. Patients undergo no inconvenience or
restraint, and are free to eat and drink what they
like.
UNQUESTIONABLE TESTIMONY.
“I had heard of the excellent results attending your
work, and was thereby induced to send my friend to you.
The results have exceeded my most sanguine expectations.
For the previous five years he has always been under the
influence cf alcohol and gradually losing power to resist
it. Since he has been under your treatment a marvellous
change has been wrought in him. Not only does he
appear to be thoroughly cured of drinking, but his
appearance, which had been degenerating, has improved,
and his color is healthy and bright. In short, the man
seems not only to have been purged of disease, but also
renewed mentally and physically.”
R. M. Me. ANDERSON, 38 Pitt-St.

April, 1894—about the worst case I ever knew—and the
effects have been most wonderful.”

BLOUSE! SHOW.
So popular a garment is the BLOUSE that vve
deem it right to assign it a position of special pro-
minence in our show of new Spring Goods.
To begin with, the BLOUSE suits the times,
because it suits the pocket, and yet so accommo-
dating is it as regards cost and quality that
BLOUSES are available at almost any price.
There is the simple homely BLOUSE of Cotton
or Muslin, and the BLOUSE of rich and handsome
Silk. To use a vulgar, though expressive, phrase,
“You Pays Your Money atnd You Takes
Your Choice. ”
For the former and simpler kind we must record
our most fervent admiration ; for the latter we have
only rapturous words of delight. There is no figure
that the BLOUSE does not suit; it is appropriate
when the waist is slim and the form slight, and
when a fair share of AVOIRDUPOIS is a notice-
able feature in the wearer. There is something of
universatility in the suitability of the BLOUSE
for all-round wear. It matters not when or where,
in the house, in the Street, out boating, on the race-
course, at the theatre, in the concert-room, at
Government House, or in poor dwellings as far
away from Vice-regal splendour as Chowder Bay
from Rotton Row.

Various Verses,
UNRECONCILED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Death wandered into my home last night,
And took my child, tho’ I held him fast; _
Tho’ I fought Death long for my heart's delight.
He conquered me in the strife at last.
And the brown eyes closed in their last deep
sleep,
And the restless hands in my hands were
still,
And someone whispered, “ Why should you
weep?
He has gone to rest—it is God’s great will.”
But I fain would weep as in days of old ;
The pagan mothers might mourn and cry
When their well-loved dead in their arms lay cold ;
Nohopes “beyond” when they said “Good-bye!”
For I am but human, my thoughts will cling
To the little child who is all alone ;
Though they tell me gently how angels bring
The infant souls to the dazzling throne.
He might lose his way in the bright star-mist,
He might call and call and I would not know,
And the smile would fade from the lips I kissed
When they took him from me so long ago.
It is but a day and it seems like years ;
The night is dark and the wind is chill;
I can only think, in my useless tears,
Of my child alone on the wind-swept hill.
For my babe is laid ’neath the new-turned sod—
I am but human, my heart is there;
And God will forgive—if there be a God
Who lays the hearts of His creatures bare.
“Weep not ! ” they whisper, he is at rest
With angels now in eternal joy ;
But I only know that my yearning breast
Will never shelter my baby boy.
Q.
THANE.
THE WORKERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You who sit with idle hands alone
In the empty splendour of your throne,
In the desert you call heaven, and dream
The fulfilment of your spacious scheme;
How your primal impulse still is surging,
Force on force converging,
Swirling suns and systems on is urging,
Till space pulses as one perfect theme !
You who hear Man’s ceaseless praises rise,
Mingled with his curses and his cries,
Waiting till from vague, discordant wrong
You shall mould the mighty spheral song !
See you not the millions of the working
In their warrens lurking,
With the grief of toil their spirits irking?
Yet upon your earth they think, live, throng 1
They have neither passions nor restraints :
They are neither sinning men nor saints.
Can you punish them or praise, when they
Have no time to blaspheme or to pray ?
They are not within your churches chanting,
Nor on footways flaunting,
But within their warren workshops, panting,
Silent, songless, toiling through the day !
Are you blind, and deaf, as well as dumb?
Do no voices through your silence come?
Is each pant and curse a dull appeal
Smothered by the snarling of the wheel?
More machine-like grows each man and woman,
Shut their massy tomb in,
While their mindless engines grow more human—
Great, gaunt, bloodless things of steel!
There are some who may repentance win,
Haloed with some sudden, splendid sin.
Look on these whom only labor drives ;
Loose a little while their leaden gyves.
You who fling strange glints and golden graces
On earth’s sombre places,
Put a little color in their faces—
Put a little color in their lives !
Maoriland.

66 Yankee Doodle , 99 blended with Polities .
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has keen built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIN SON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands: —“CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE'
ranks supreme.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not. open to residents of Sydney. In that
city Tub Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed I. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Ami picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
pai l for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

After the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 26, 1895.
Praying for Rain: Second Chapter.
(1) Is it a fact that N.S.W. Executive recently set apart
a day for humiliation and prayer for rain ?
(2) If so, was the experiment satisfactory?
(3) If so, will the Government take into consideration
the fact that no rain of any importance has fallen in the
Western District, and in view of the great distress pre-
vailing on account of the long-continued drought in the
Western part of this country will the Government take
into consideration the advisability of gazetting a special
day for humiliation and prayer for rain for the Western
District only ? Question by Richard Sleatii,
disallowed by Speaker Abbott.
A few weeks ago the province of New South
Wales was suffering badly from a general
want of rain. Therefore the Government,
incited by the clerical element of the com-
munity and by Education Minister Jacob
Garrard, who says that he and Ins col-
leagues are “a Goo-fearing Ministry,”
appointed a day of prayer and humiliation
for the essential moisture. The event was
fixed for a Sunday, apparently because the
Government hadn’t enough faith in the
experiment to lose a day’s work over it,
and it wasn’t fixed till three State Astro-
nomers had announced that rain might be
expected. There was no special humiliation
about the proceeding; the female ele-
ment of the community wore its best
frock as usual instead of going to
church in sackcloth and with bare feet
and ashes on its head, and the male element
looked the same as it does at other times—
in fact, there was no visible humiliation. In
the olden days, when people had still suffi-
cient faith in their creed to be willing to
look ridiculous for its sake, they would pro-
bably have humiliated in rags, and on all-
fours, and with their hair in a state of weird
disarrangement, but that kind of thing
belongs to a bygone era. Also the popula-
tion didn’t pray more than usual, and there
was hardly any noticeable increase in the
congregations, which are small at the best of
times. So that the situation is that nobody
humiliated and only about one-sixth of the
population went to church ; and of that
fraction the Catholics didn't pray for rain at
all -their prayers being fixed for the ensuing
month, so that the Catholic rain might he
kept distinct from the Protestant rain and
the exceedingly small fraction that remained
didn’t pray directly, but only at second-
hand through its pastor. In short, there
was a great and woeful lack of energy about
the proceeding.
* # *
Nevertheless the rain came. In the
eastern part of the province, where droughts
are rare and of short continuance, and where
the downfall was less wanted, it came abun-
dantly. In the great, dry West, out
“where the Dead Men lie,” and where
droughts are long and dreadful, and rain
was most wanted, it came moderately in
some places, sparingly in others, while in
many districts it hardly arrived at all. But
the people of Sydney, whose ignorance of
the western regions is vast and pitiable,
assumed that because it sloshed down plenti-
fully in Pitt-street and Woollooraooloo, the
drought had definitely broken up, and the
Church thereupon began to suffer from
swelled head over its success, and the
Cabinet announced that’ it was “a God-
fearing Ministry,” and reached out for the
pious vote on account of the rain, and a day
of thanksgiving was held for the great bless-
ing that had partly arrived. And even while
the thanksgiving was in progress the thirst-
maddened cattle were still dying in the far
west; and the gaunt horses still dropped by
the wayside; and the drought, in many
localities, was very much as before.
• * *
Therefore, Mr. Slbath’s question was per-
fectly natural and logical. The churches
asked Providence to work a miracle on
their behalf. They assumed that without
their prayers the rain would not have
arrived, therefore its arrival was a miracle,
and they returned thanks on that assump-
tion. Also, they suffered from the afore-
mentioned attack of swelled head on the
same assumption. Even the fact that the
rain came mostly where it usually comes
without any supernatural interference, and
that it stayed away mostly in the
x’egions where it generally stays away, and
where, by reason of its customary staying
away it was most wanted, and that it fell also
in Queensland and Victoria where there had
been no day of humiliation and prayer, made
no difference to the clerical mind. Assum-
ing, therefore, that Providence had directly
interfered in the matter, it is obviously quite
as much within the power of the Infinite to
send lavish rains all over the dry plains of
the West, if asked to do so. It is also quite
as much within its power to remove the
curse of poverty and want of work, and to
cause a great revival of trade which will
supply employment and wages for all who
want them. Man has found remedies for
want of rain in irrigation and artesian wells,
but no one has yet found even the semblance
of an adequate and practicable cure for the
ever-prevalent want of employment ; there-
fore the latter is a much fitter subject for
prayer than the former. Rain always comes
sooner or later, prayer or no prayer, so no
one can say for certain that its coming is the
result of prayer. Abundant work and a
living wage for everybody never come—at
least, never within the memory of man —so
their coming after prayer would be almost a
sure sign of divine interference. Yet the
churches, with this splendid opening for
demonstrating the efficacy of prayer and
for showing their faith in their own creed,
never set apart a special occasion for prayers
for work and good wages. They always lift
the light end of the log and leave the heavy
end carefully alone.
* * #
The question raised by Sleath, M.L.A.,
was, however, a less difficult one than this.
He simply asked (h it, if the Government
felt assured, as it j ro essed (per medium of
Minister Garrard; to be, that the rain which
recently fell in the damp parts of the pro-
vince was an answer to prayer, it would pray
again for rain in the dry regions where the
supply is still deficient. As"it rains occasion-
ally in these parts, even in the worst year, he
did not ask for anything very extraordinary.
Yet his request met with a cold reception.
The question was first disallowed for no
visible reason, and when at last he did con-
trive to bring the matter before the House
he received an evasive and unsatisfactory
reply. Since then the Government has
done nothing further in the matter ; neither
have the churches ; neither has anybody
else. The clergy, having succeeded in bring-
ing down abundance of rain in a region
where it comes without prayer, or having
been in the vicinity when it came according
to the natural course of events, are appar°
ently not prepared to risk their sudden repu-
tation by trying to bring it down in a region
where it only comes occasionally either
through prayer or cursing or by any other
natural or unnatural means. Nor 'has The
Bulletin’s suggestion of a fortnight ago, that
there should be set apart a day of humilia-
tion and prayer for work and good wages,
evoked any response. Yet no believer can
deny that the one evil is as easily remedied by
the Almighty as the other, if the Almighty
does answer prayer by spasmodic interfer-
ences with the fixed order of Creation. This
world is a very, very small affair—the mere
shadow of a speck of dust on the outer rim
of infinity ; and all the doings on its surface
are only “ a trouble of ants in the light of a
million million of suns.’ If the theory of
Divine interference is correct, it is such a
very small affair to set right all that problem
of poverty and stagnation for which man can
find no workable remedy—it is asking an
.easier thing from the Omnipotent than if we
asked the greatest earthly autocrat to order
his minions to command somebody to dot
the “i” in Pakkes’s worst poem. And the
churches believe in the necessity of it; and
they likewise believe, or profess to believe,
in the existence of their Deity ; and they
fully believe, or profess to believe, that
He constantly departs from what would
otherwise be the fixed order of the universe
in order to answer human appeals for aid.
And yet the suggestion that they should
specially supplicate for honest work and
honestly-earned wages evokesj no response.
They keep on praying for the thing that is
dead-sure to come soon in any case ; but
they won’t pray (as proposed by Sleath)
for the thing that generally comes late ; and
still less will they pray for the thing which,
in the ordinary course of events, doesn’t
come at all.
• # #
The church, in fact, has little faith, or no
faith at all, in its own creed, and it takes no
avoidable risks. Still, it is burdened with
certain risks which were handed down to it
by its more reckless forefathers. Therefore,
it petitions regularly according to set formu-
las to be delivered from battle, murder, and
sudden death, and all three continue to exist
much as before. It asks for deliverance
from pestilence, and in the cities where men
are clean and pious, and also where they
are clean and not pious, there is no pestil-
ence, and where they are pious and not
clean, or where they are neither clean nor
pious, there is pestilence with the usual
regularity. Also, the Church has prayed for
many, many years that all the nobility may
be endowed with grace, wisdom and under-
standing, and the only result, so far, is that
part of the nobility is endowed with under-
standing, and part of it is only endowed with
money made out of breweries, and a good
part is endowed with nothing in particular
save a yearning to wed some painted Flossie
of the ballet. Also, it prays constantly
that all magistrates may execute justice and
maintain truth, yet the J.P. is as foolish and
objectionable as usual, and shows no sign of
improvement. In all these matters many
long years of supplication have brought no
answer, but the public has grown so used to
hearing the old, stereotyped impossible
appeal that it has forgotten what the appeal
is about, or forgotten to expect an answer,
or forgotten to wonder why the answer
never comes. So the Church still professes
to believe in the efficacy of prayer, and its
followers never seem to notice the awful dis-
crepancy ot things.
* » m
The whole question resolves itself, so far
as all available evidence goes, somewhat as
follows,: The world exists ; no one knows
exactly where it came from, or how it came
from there, or where it is going to, or how
it will get there, but its existence, at all
events, seems reasonably certain. If the
churches think they know where it came
from or where it is going to, they have a
perfect right to do so. It is governed by
certain laws ; if the churches think they
know who made these laws, that is their own
affair exclusively. When man has found out
all these laws, he will probably be able
to abolish droughts, floods, bad crops,
disease, and most of his other troubles,
and then it will be a beautiful and
harmonious globe ; or, at all events, it
will be much more beautiful and harmonious
than it is at present. Until ho finds out
these laws he has to take the consequences
of his ignorance. The machine never stops
to take account of anyone. It makes no
allowance for anybody’s ignorance. No
extraneous power that can be"appealed to by
prayer, or the pounding of tom-toms, or the
burning of incense, or by abasement before
Joss, or by hymns, or drums, or any other
kind of discord ever stops the wheels for one
instant, or varies the course of events by one
hair s-breadth. Therefore, it seems that
all the sects on earth may pray for
ever for rain, or the abolition of pestil-
ence, or for the endowment of the
nobility with wisdom, but unless they first
find out why rain is scarce, and pestilence
exists, and the nobility is so very short of
wisdom, they will get no further. And if
the sects are not yet convinced of this, and
if they really believe in their own profes-
sions, then let them try an experiment by
praying unanimously, not for something that
always comes in any case, but for something
that is very much wanted and which never
yet has come. The public is ready enough
—much too ready, in fact—to believe in
supernatural interferences; all it wants is
one such interference that can stand reason-
able inspection.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tree Postage and Free Telegrams.
Maoriland post and telegraph department
yields for the year just closed a net profit of
.£57,478. The New South Wales service, which
has twice the revenue, shows a loss of ,£123,967,
or, allowing for interest on money spent for con-
struction, a loss of ,£222,030. A good part of
this amount, which the province makes good
out of taxation, is the cost of carrying news-
papers free of postage by way of granting Pro-
tection to the Sydney press against country
and foreign papers. Every Sydney morning
paper which goes into the country to explain
the beauties and advantages of Freetrade is
protected to the extent of 50 percent. And
not one shoddy journal lias yet raised its voice
against tin's iniquity, for Freetrade is alwavs
intended solely for the other man. The spec-
tacle of the Herald and Telegraph and
other papers, not forgetting The Bulle-
tin, sponging on a" department which
is so utterly bankrupt as this is
sordid and disgusting, and is suggestive of a
wealthy capitalist stealing a copper from a
blind beggar. Meanwhile a new aspect of the
situation is threatening. Free newspaper-
postage is only beneficial to papers which cir-
culate largely outside their own postal dis-
tricts. The hungry, little country “rag,”*with
a small circulation within a very narrow area,
gets no benefit from it whatever ; it circulates,
as a rule, in one locality only and not very
much there, and the owner has to deliver his
production by hand as heretofore. Meanwhile
the city paper, which is much larger and
more up-to-date than its poor bush rival, is
brought up by train, or coach, or steamer, or
mounted mailman, at the cost of the taxpayer,
and distributed by the postman, also at the
cost of the taxpayer, so that the country journal
is crushed out even on its own ground. There-
fore the scribe of the ’possum and native-
bear region now demands that he should
get his news telegrams free, which means
more cost to the taxpayer, to restore the
balance. P.M.G. Cook replied in the House
that this was wholly impossible, but the
bush editor will probably stick to his point and
keep on agitating, and though the influence of
one little smudgy paper published in the town
of Goo-forgotten is almost nil, the influence of
many dozens of such organs is very appreci-
able. By-and-bye, the aggregate country
editor will become solid on the question, and
his attitude will be that he must either get
his news telegrams free or he will be con-
stantly and consistently “agin” the Govern-
ment, just as the city editor is “ agin ” every
Government which threatens to stop his free
postage. And then the State must either
abolish the present free postage scandal or else
add the free telegram scandal to it to make
things square. And as the State’s present
rulers haven’t pluck or honesty or brains
enough to abolish the existing iniquity they
will probably cave in and pacify the country
papers, and after that the city papers will
want their news telegrams gratis also, and there
will be a really good time ahead for the huge
and unnecessary New South Wales post and
telegraph deficiency.
More Syndicatism.
A bill is to be brought suddenly before N.S.W.
Parliament in the last days of the session,
when the Assembly is tired and demoralised,
authorising a “syndicate” to constructan under-
ground electric railway from Sydney to North
Shore, ihis scheme, it is alleged, has been in
preparation for two years, but it has been pre-
pared in an atmosphere of such strict secresy
that, till last Monday, hardly a single person
outside the syndicate knew of its existence.
The proposed line is to start from about the cor-
ner of George and King streets and connect with
Milson’s Point. It will cost an enormous
amount of money—British money presumably—
and it may or may not supply a long-felt want,
according to circumstances. At the same time,
there are many good reasons why it should be
remorselessly biffed out of Parliament as a
subtle and dangerous fraud upon posterity, and
every M. L. A. who votes for it, or fails to vote
against it, should be called to account by bis
constituents. The question at issue is not
whether the spec, would or would not be a
paying concern ; it might return huge dividends
or it might never pay 1 per cent on the capital
sunk, but it is equally objectionable either way.
North Sydney will ultimately be a great city,
with a large suburban population spread all
over the space between Lane Cove and
Middle Harbor, and on the other side of Mid-
dle Harbor towards Manly. This population
will be served by the Government railway and
tramway as far as Milson’s Point. On the other
side the city railway must ultimately be ex-
tended to the water’s edge, and when this is
done the syndicate proposes to hold the con-
necting-link between the two systems. When
the State line is carried right through the city,
then that, and not the present awkward devia-
tion via Strathfield, will be the direct route
from Adelaide to Brisbane - the greatest
national railway in Australia, and one of the
greatest in the world. But there will be a
cumbersome and absurd gap in the route ; it
will be a great State line through four pro-
vinces and over 2000 miles of country—all ex-
cept about one mile of private line which will
intervene in the busiest part of the route !
The Government may, of course, make its own
connecting link if it pleases, but, in the first
place, the syndicate will hold the best and
shortest route, and the only two good landing
places—Dawes Point on one side and Milson’s
Point on the other ; and its occupation of the
latter site will make it practically impossible
for the Government to connect its two lines un-
less it moves the North Sydney station to an
altogether new site. Milson’s Point is a narrow
spot where there isn’t sufficient space for two
submarine railways to emerge side by side.
Further, if the Goverment made a longer and
more costly line by some other track it would
still lose much local traffic by the competition
of the private railway, and, amid all these
troubles, the chances are that the State would
be glad to buy out the syndicate at a very
high figure and be rid of it. The fact that the
said syndicate—though it professes to have
been incubating for two years—only rushed for-
ward when it became known that the Govern-
ment was really thinking of tunnelling the har-
bor, suggests that it exists chiefly for the pur-
pose of getting a concession gratis from the
State and then selling it back again at a big
price. Possibly it wouty never build anv line
at all. By the time it had got hold of the best
tunnel site the Government would probably
awake to the situation, and would give the co.
a great many thousands to get out of the road
and not build its railway at all.
Legal Privilege.
4 What will they do about O’Halloran ? ”
was a general query in Melbourne when the
police-raid on Oxenham and Abrahams’
(betting) office got into print. The misguided
hand of the law had actually captured a
solicitor along with four professional gamblers !
The public soon learned that the lawyer was a
privileged visitor to the “ common gaming-
house.” The Crown Prosecutor, it was an-


nounced, “ did not desire to proceed against
Mr. o’Hallor.an. That gentleman w’as only
charged with being on die premises, and was
prepared to swear that he went there on pro-
fessional business and nob with the object of
gaming. He had met his client, Mr. Abra-
hams, at the Commercial Hotel, and merely
followed him into his office to conduct the busi-
ness between them more conveniently.” The
nature of the business discussion was nob
stated, but its grave importance may ho
assumed from the fact that it began in a
pub. and was continued in the alleged
gaming-house where bets were made on Caul-
field Guineas morning. Under the circum-
stances Magistrate Panton could merely beam
upon O’Halloran, and express surprise at such
a well-known lawyer being “ caught on the
premises.” Now, were the said O’Halloran
other than a lawyer he might have accompanied
Abrahams for the most innocent and respect-
able of purposes, and would yet have failed to
escape prosecution. Brown, the tailor, or
Jones, the draper, might, in such a case, tell
his true tale of legitimate business to a Crown-
Prosecutor’s deaf ear, more especially if Brown
or Jones were an inveterate backer of horses.
The law has a habit of supposing that every
person captured in a gaming-house is there for
an unlawful purpose, unless the person be a
solicitor. Mr. O’Halloran’s profession saved
him from the misfortune of unjust suspicion
and prosecution. There is, seemingly, no end
to a lawyer’s list of privileges. Incriminating
circumstances roll away from his char-
acter like water from a duck’s back.
But, to return to the bould O’Halloban. As
soon as the lawyer’s satisfactory explanation
was made known to the court, bookmaker
Sammy Allen spoke up for his innocence.
Said swarthy Samuel : “ I’m placed in the
same predicament as Mr. Halloran (laughter).
I simply went into the shop. I’m not interested
in Abrahams’ shop. I just met him outside ”
(laughter). The bookmaker’s excuse was rather
more convincing, on the face of it, than the
other gentleman’s excuse, bookies being
gregarious animals, who frequent one another’s
betting-shops in a casual sorb of way. Anyway,
the unwisdom of worrying Allen (whilst allow-
ing the lawyer to clear himself so easily) must
have been apparent to Panton, P.M., for
Samuel, though remanded with the other sus-
pects, was not required to find bail.
The triangular duel for the N.S.W. Legis-
lative Assembly seat which Meagher lost
through breathing his gruesome tale into
the ear of Salomons resulted in the return of
sturdy old Henry Copeland by a majority of
120 votes. The Protectionist candidate gob 423
votes ; the Foreign-trader, 303 ; and the Labor
candidate, 163. Consequently, the Foreign-
trade papers now point out that there is in
Phillip Division a large Government majority,
and that the seat was jumped by a minority
representative through the sinful cussedness of
the Labor man in splitting the Foreign-trade
vote. This may be, and, on the other hand, it
may not be ; it is mere assumption in any case.
The Foreign-trade vote represents the straight
Government party ; the vote which put Cope-
land in represents the party which constantly
opposes the Government; and the Labor-vote
represents the party which says that the
Government is a hypocritical crowd that
hates democratic principles and would not even
attempt democratic legislation if it were not
driven along by the Labor members under pain
of losing its miserable salary. The Labor-party
states, in effect, that the Opposition is its open
and unscrupulous enemy and the Government
its false friend. Under these conditions the
cheerful assumption that it there had been no
Labor candidate the 163 men who voted for
Wilson would have voted for Hodgson instead
is a tolerably impudent affair. In any case,
New South Wales badly wants the second
ballot, partly to prevent minority representa-
tion and partly to shut up the wolfish howl of
the Telegraph that whenever its party is
defeated it loses through the splitting up of
the vote.
When the Land-tax becomes law in N.S. Wales,
there will be an excellent opportunity to pass
an Act decreeing that all land resumed by the
State for railways, roads, or other public pur-
poses shall be taken over at Land-tax valuation
with, say, 10 per cent, added for “ disturbance.”
The tax will provide a reliable valuation for all
estates above the exemption limit, and it will
also supply a partial valuation for estates below
that limit; in other words, it will prove that
their value does not exceed the amount of the
exemption, whatever that may be. It would
be a glaring and ridiculous scandal for the
Treasury to collect the tax on an estate at the
valuation of £2 an acre for many years, and then
suddenly find itself ordered by the usual pig-
headed jury to pay £lO an acre for the same
property when it is w’anted for railway purposes.
And yet that is exactly what will happen unless
some steps are taken to exclude the pig-headed
jury from the matter altogether. If Govt,
will adopt the simple and equitable plan above
suggested, then the only place where the old
swindle can come in is in the valuation of
fences, buildings, and other improvements, and
an ordinary post-and-rail fence doesn’t offer the
same facilities for stupendous lying as the
land-question does. It is true that a Vic. rail-
way commissioner once paid £4O or £5O com-
pensation for one small tree, on the ground that
it was much prized by the family and its
removal was calculated to lacerate the owner’s
feelings, but that was exceptional. Not one
landowner in a hundred has the financial genius
to exact a small fortune in compensation for
one tree and some alleged “feelings.”
The Queensland rabbit is a most adaptive
animal. He can exist on any kind of vegetation
found in the South-West except the needle-bush,
and on this he is at present experimenting. He has
learnt to swim, to climb, and (according to some
authorities) to jump about in the trees like a
’possum. At present he is desperately trying to
adapt himself to a diet of phosphorus, sugar and
pollard, but so far with indifferent suco. a. He
doesn’t increase and multiply everywhere a some
suppose; 25 years ago rabbits were inmerous on
someof thestationsontheGladstone-Maryii f ugh
road, but not a trace of them is now to lie found.
Perhaps some bush naturalist can explain why.

The most attractive Australian-produced
colored advts, which The Bulletin has seen are
those issued by John Sands on behalf of Dixson’s
No. 1 cigarettes. The coming woman and the
black cat take a prominent place. The pictures
are obviously from the cunning hand of Souter.
The French have captured Antananarivo.
A few weeks ago, when they were nearing that
capita], it was cabled that the missionaries were
not going to bolt from the city ; they “ preferred
to die at their posts.” This childish “ blow ” was
sent out all over the world, and all the mild old
ladies who keep png dogs and drink afternoon tea
field up their mittened hands in admiration at the
stupendous valor of the holy men who thus looked
death in the eye. Of course, the French troops
didn’t kill any missionaries, and no holy man
died at his post or ever expected to; and things
are just the same in Antananarivo, save that the
French have probably cleaned up the city a good
deal and invited the preachers to dinner.
The modern magazine-rifle seems likely to
be a failure, after all. The bullets are needle-like
in shape, and carry for immense distances, but
they go right through the enemy without causing
immediate disablement, unless they happen to
penetrate the brain or heart. In a recent Indian
campaign a gruesome story is told of a nigger who
was shot through by six separate bullets, three
of them in the body, and was finally captured
alive and still fighting. He recovered. Even
when the bullet strikes a bone it seldom breaks it.
In most cases it simply drills a small and very
neat hole right through it, and passes on, leaving
the patient still in fighting condition. It looks as
if the modern fire-arm has been improved too
much, and it may yet become necessary to hit out
with the butt-end, after all.
A Bathurst man who attended a recent
meeting of London unemployed, at Nelson’s
Monument, writes : —“lt was about the hollowest
affair of the kind I ever saw. The chief speakers
were Tom Mann, Ben Tillett, a socialistic local
preacher, and a Lancashire lass. Tom Mann, a
powerful-looking fellow, with an earnest, toil-
worn face, made a speech that breathed nothing
but fiery revolution. He advised the starving un-
employed to sack the butchers’ and bakers’ shops,
which evoked a feeble cheer. But the sight of a
few stalwart policemen, leisurely pacing round
the outskirts of the crowd, seemed to exercise a
deadening influence. When someone called out,
‘ Six o’clock ; I’m goin’to my dinner,’there was
a chorus of laughter. The idea of starving men
having a dinner to go to seemed to strike them as
a good joke. The crowd broke up, and in a few
minutes had melted away.”
“ Here ” (exclaims a Bulletin correspon-
dent in Western N.S.W.) “here is Minister
Garrard now thanking God that the drought has
broken up. It hasn’t. There hasn’t been a decent
shower yet within 100 miles of here. country
was never in a worse state. Stock are dying by
thousands. And, as for the rabbits —they are
literally heaped up outside the wire-netting
around the dams. If this God-fearing Ministry
doesn’t supplicate for us, there’ll be trouble.
Anyway, ‘Lands’ Carruthers ought. The re-
appraisement of runs and selections is just going
to be made, and if rain doesn’t fall soon down the
rents will go with an awful thud, and, of course,
will stay there for from seven to fourteen years.
As Treasurer, Reid ought also tg supplicate—in a
strictly business way. He’s ‘ the chap to save the
country,’ you know. If we can’t move the
N.S.W. Ministry, we’ll have to see what Kingston
can do in the matter.”
During the last few years great numbers
of horses which have grazed in the lower Darling
River country have become blind. After local
vets, had been baffled in their attempts to dis-
cover the cause and a remedy, Govt, officers were
sent out, and their reports, published two months
ago, were just as inconclusive. Subsequently, Dr.
Harvey, of Wentworth, theorises that the disease
was due to the transference of microbes from the
excreta of rabbits to the horses. Settlers, how-
ever, will have none of that theory. They say
they have wire-netted paddocks, killed the rabbits,
and then ploughed the ground, and afterwards
horses grazed thereon have gone blind. Some of
the settlers say that wild melons—the prickly
little “squashes”—are the cause; others that it
is the native tobacco-plant. The difficulty of
determining lies in the fact that all three—rabbits,
melons, and tobacco-plant generally flourish
together. Meantime it is agreed that the only
safe plan is to feed stock off the low sandy flats.
Already, however, hundreds of horses have become
wholly or partially blind.
“Did you ever see a standing man asleep ?
It is on record,” writes a Bulletin correspondent,
“that during the course of some of the ‘little’
South African wars, sentries (black) were found
soundly sleeping with legs widely separated, the
butt of the rifle on the ground, the arms folded on
the muzzle and the head laid on the arms.
I have walked by the side of a man so
fatigued that he slept as he trudged, and was only
awakened by colliding with the hedge at a turn of
the road, I saying nothing, just to see what the
sleeper would do. It is a common thing for bush-
men to sleep in the saddle, but in that case there
is no necessity for muscular rigidity of the legs.
Also, while voyaging between Tasmania and
England years ago, on one hot night in the
tropics, though not in the doldrums, I found the
officer of the watch, the apprentice, the man at
the wheel and the look-out all fast asleep, and the
barque “sailing herself.” I have likewise seen,
on a coasting steamer between Brisbane and
Sy Iney, the watch-officer standing in the corner
of his canvas-shelter dozing, the steersman with
his head on his folded arms on the wheel (steam-
gearing), and the lookout-man unmistakeably
asleep ; the resl of the watch making no pretence
of wakefulness. In the latter case it is not so
much the fault of the men as of the system, which
keeps them hard at work in port all day, and then
sends them on duty for the first watch without a
chance of resting. Under such circumstances men
often simply cannot keep awake—though it is only
fair to say that, in the case mentioned, a very
slight noise, such as a footfall on the bridge, was
enough to aroqse the sleepers. Still, given no
footfall, with a 16-knot ship, it does not require a
great space of time for her to be miles out of her
course; and given also a rock, with or without a
light, you have all the material for a first-class
wreck.”

There is a lighthouse-keeper on North
Coast of N.S.W. who, forever 20 years, has not
been 10 miles away from his post. His 10 chil-
dren have been born there, and, until the last
month, have had to go four miles to school.
In Walhalla(Gippsland, Yic.) region, where
much timber-felling is done, about 80 percent, of
the axemen are Italians (considered by many
people to be the best axemen in the world). They
are the personification of industry—working about
10 hours in winter and quite 14 hours a day in
summer. They live almost entirely on the fattest
bacon obtainable, bread and wine.
From a N.S.W. backblocks letter:—
“ Eight weeks on a hungry track. Got a week’s
work at last for a blacksmith cocky —such a nice
man. Have no particular hours—just from sun-
rise to sunset. Have just got my wages—a pair
of good pusher boots (second-hand) and a 2d.
stamp, which same is on this letter. Am off
again this morning to make my fortune.”
Have any Bulletin readers on this side
the continent seen the Westralian native potatoes
—“yook”? They grow along the salt lakes, in
loose sand. The plant is a tiny bush, and the
potatoes grow around it in a circle of from 15in.
to 18in. in radius. From one plant you may get
half a bagful. Look like lemons, and have a
peculiar smell. The pulp tastes like carrots, only
not so strong. They are succulent, and quench
thirst admirably.
Bringing up a child in a public-house
sharpens his wits. A few Sundays since, service
was being held on a Northern N.S.W. river, in a
hall near the hotel. The landlord’s little son,
barefooted, ragged and very dirty, strolled up to
the door, and, seeing the priest, a great favorite
of his, walked in and took a seat by his friend.
The collection-box having been passed round, the
child waited till the money was poured on the
table in front of the clergyman, and then, pluck-
ing his reverence by the sleeve, the son of his
father whispered—“ Say, shall I run and tell dad
you are going to have a tarnbooroora ? ”
Dear Bulletin, — I a|feo have cured a
horse of jibbing. In this way.’ The brute stopped
in the middle of a sheet of water, about 100yds.
wide and 2ft. deep. A tentative application of
the whip made him look as if he would kick. I
was alone, so that the idea of taking him out and
walking him up and down through the water
would not do, as no one would have been left to
take care of the buggy. So I lit up a small fire—
not under him, but about 6ft. or 7ft. behind him—
in a pipe. Then I fell asleep. When I awoke, we
were out of that water, and about two miles nearer
homo. This is the best plan I have ever tried.—
Horse-traineb.
The S.A. Local Option Bill, now before
Parliament, provides that one-tenth of the rate-
payers in any district may demand a poll on the
liquor question. One-fourth of the ratepayers
must vote to make the poll valid ; a bare majority
of the votes may decide that no new licences will
be granted, or that the existing licences may be
reduced by one-third or even two-thirds, while a
two-thirds majority of the votes may enforce abso-
lute Prohibition. A vote either way is definite
for three years. Meanwhile, no one seems to
know how the State will make up the loss of the
drink income when Prohibition arrives. Nearly
.£2,000,000 a year of beautiful revenue will go
bung with the arrival of the Cold-Tea epoch, and
it will probably cost another half-million to hunt
after and convict people suspected of making
illicit grog in the cellar.
When the Local Option Bill was booming
merrily through Maoriland Parliament, one hard-
headed M.H.R. arose to ask how, when the
accursed drink was abolished, they were going to
replace the liquor-revenue of £440,000 per annum.
No one answered, and the Bill went on bootnii g
as before. The one fact that the teetotaller
declines to face is that the liquor-revenue in Aus-
tralasia amounts to 15s. or £1 per head of the
population, and that when the unclean thing is
abolished this will have to be made up in some
other way. And whereas the liquor money comes
only from the drinking section of the community,
the new taxation will probably hit all classes
alike, so that the Cold Tea man will have to pay a
lot more taxes than he does at present. This will
be especially rough on the teetotal lecturer ; he
will not only have to pay more taxes, but he will
lose a good paying billet for want of any more
drink to lecture about.
A great effort is going to be made in
Maoriland Parliament to procure the exemption
from legacy duty of all bequests for religious pur-
poses and for the encouragement of literature and
the fine arts. The idea is to avoid payment of
£12,000 duty on a pile which a deceased citizen
has bequeathed for pious purposes. The church
is an exceedingly tough subject for taxation. It
already dodges land and income taxes, and it is
always yearning after more exemptions. And yet
no valid reason has ever been advanced why the
church should not pay on exactly the same basis
as the public-house and the grocery and the pig-
farm ; it only avoids payment because the average
legislator has a weak, hazy idea that if he aggra-
vates the tabernacle in this world something bad
may happen to him in the next, and he knows
that nothing makes the tabernacle boil over so
dreadfully as parting with money. The church is
the biggest and most persistent deadhead on
earth, and the best that can be said for it is that
the pig-farming business or the ready-made boot
interest would be quite as aggressive if it had the
same superstition at its back.
Edgar’s story, “Te Arero Maori,” sug-
gests to a Bulletin correspondent the fact that
another attempt to establish a Maori newspaper is
just now being made in Wellington (M.L.). A
local firm of printers are in it, likewise a half-caste
native agent and interpreter, who has already
begun a canvass. R. S. Thompson, a journalist
well-known in M.L., is to be editor, with an
interest in the venture. If varied experience is a
qualification for the editorship of a Maori news-
paper. R.S.T. should fill the bill. Son of a clergy-
man (Eng.), a soldier in the Maori war, West
Coast miner when Premier Seddon was a store-
keeper in a very small way, a pakeha-Maori, who, -
by his persistency in re native affairs, has made
every officer in the M.L. Lands Office dread the
mention of the name of Queenie Thompson
(R.S.F.’s “quarter-caste” wife, who owns land
that has been in dispute for a quarter of a cen-
tury), and who tells the best stories of Hori Grey
to be heard in Maoriland, he ought certainly to be
able to run the as yet unnamed paper. Te Waka
Maori (Maori Canoe) went bung, though (or
because?) run by Govt. Private enterprise is now
to have a show —always providing the Maories are
not too lazy to pay up. 40,000 Maories and their
friends should surely be able to support one paper
among them. The latest proposal is to run it as
a half-caste print—half in each language.

I’ve done my whack.
In sin and disepation.
As A gambler and thief.
I have bean chief.
With all its degridation.
But I hope I am one.
Whoes name will be John.
Now ive seen my sin and folley.
For as gambler and thief.
Has brought me to grief.
In it there is nothing so jolley.
Aii the lad is A fool.
That practises the rule.
Of trickery and deception.
For it is sure to bring.
Him an unpleaseut sling.
By way of A fools correction.
THE CONVICT IN CHAINES.
In the happy days of childhood.
Of innocence and bliss.
Did I ever think that manhood.
Would bring such A curse as thiss.
Then I was free from laws alarms.
Its perils and its pains.
But now I am in A foreign land.
A poor convict in chaines.
I once was young and happy.
In A mothers fond embrace.
But now 1 am old and shabby.
And in the very worst disgrace.
Bub the cause is all ray own.
By seeking dishonist gaines.
I am in A dungeon thrown.
A poor convict in chaines.
Oh how I wish that I had bean.
A virtueous lionist lad.
I should not have such trouble sean.
Enough to drive me mad.
I should lie free and happy now.
From all my galling paines.
Instead of in A prison cell.
A poor convict in chaines.
But happy am I that now I can see.
Before it is too late.
That even now I can prepear.
To avoid A Ur mo.e wretchard fate.
May God cive me grace to (nay.
To av ide salans hurtling pains.
And make me happy in ct. rnily.
Free from sin and sorrows chaines.
