George Leitch’s great spectacle, “The
Land of the Moa,” at Sydney Royal, is even a
more gorgeous affair than previous reports seemed
to indicate. From a literary point of view it is
moderately respectable up to the end of the third
act, and after that it is a weird whirl and whiz of
miscellaneous glitter and horror, so that it doesn’t
matter whether it is respectable or not. The plot
is apparently an adaptation of Besant’s tale,
“Over the Sea with a Sailor.” There is the same
girl, and her artist lover, and her bad uncle who
is a pilot, only lie is called Samson Noblem instead
of Stephen Cobbledick; and the same garish
Yankee captain entices the girl on board bis ship
with her uncle’s assistance and carries her away
across the sea. Then he is thunder-blasted by the
appearance on board of bis former girl, who is a
Maori this time instead of a quadroon ; and be is
chased by a British gunboat instead of a Federal
cruiser; and wrecked in Maoriland in place of
Virginia; and the two girls are turned loose among
the natives instead of among the riff-raff of the
borderland. And, finally, the artist rescues them
just like Besant’s artist, and the wicked captain
is thrown into a geyser and boiled, whereas the
original wicked captain was thrown into Niagara;
and the maundering old pilot charges his nieee
with ingratitude and points out that he found her
on a raft in the Bay of Bengal, whereas the
original Cobbledick states that he found his niece
on a raft in the same place, wrapped in 44 ban-
danna handkerchiefs.
• # *
The co. is a long way above the usual melo-
dramatic level. George Leitcb’s pilot is a strange,
comic, snuffling old man who is a most interesting
study. The captain, who would be a shrieking
pirate under worse circumstances, is made by
Dacre a fine, robust, affable, gentlemanly cut-
throat, whom it is a pleasure to meet; and Miss
Amy Roselle’s Maori girl is a soul-satisfying
effort. Mi»s Polly Emery is another aboriginal
lady who helps the dtaina along materially, and
Cathcart, Keightley, and Frank Hawthorne do
some respectable work in between the various
explosions. The “ Land of the Moa ” is a most
explosive drama, and full of beauteous scenery.
Nearly every historic beauty-spot in Maoriland
is thrown in regardless of expense—geysers,
craters, and hot springs are quite common,
and the Pink Terraces of Rotomaiiana are a
dream of unearthly splendor. The volcano has
an act all to itself, and through four scenes it
explodes, and blasts around, and fills the country
with fire and ashes and great chunks of desolation,
and tears the hills asunder, and most of the co. is
buried among tlie ruins. Asa high-class family
explosion this eruption takes the cake. A whole
lot of picturesque and blood-curdling Maori dances
are also scattered through tlie drama, and the
whoops of the natives, and their songs, and the
bounding of their dark brown legs make up a
spectacle such as the Australian stage has never
seen before. Also, it isn’t likely ever to see it
again, for the Maori is sinking into a beastly state
of civilisation and death, and soon there won’t be
one really picturesque yell left in Maoriland.
* » » *
The comic opera season at Sydney Lyceum
ends to-night (Timrsday) with a bill made
up pieces of “ Paul Jones,” “ Gondoliers,”
“The Mountebanks,” “Pinafore,” and “ Ma
Mie Rosette.” Public opinion concerning the new
songstress is left in a much-divided state. Miss
Wray has certainly lost ground during the latter
half of tiie season. She made a decided hit in
“Madame Angot ” and in “Nitouche.” Then
she fell away considerably in “ Pinafore,” where
her Josephine was hopelessly eclipsed by that of
Mias Florence Young, whose voice is always
sweet and beautiful, and her acting good,
even if it falls short of actual brilliance. In the
“Mikado” Miss WTay’s Yiim-Ynin was accept-
able in many respects, yet the Katisba of Florence
Young decidedly overshadowed it. Opinion
concerning Miss Juliet is now in a dazed and
shattered condition, but the general idea is that
she can act a few parts well and a number of
others only moderately, and that her voice is not
wholly reliable, and, in short, that she is a lady of
possibilities who may become a very fine actress,
or only an erratic one. At present she is an un-
finished meteor.
* * * *
A dramatic season begins at the Lyceum
on Saturday, the first production being “ In Old
Kentucky,” which is now doing a triumphant
career in the Land of the Wooden Ham. Theco.
is a strong one, including Miss Helen Dauvray,
the well-known American actress ; these two
attract! ve and deservedly popular Australiennes,
Misses Harrie Ireland and Nelly Mortyne ; also,
King Hedley (he of the great horse-sensation at
Her Majesty’s), little Ivy Scott, D'Orsay Ogden,
Elton, Scot Inglis, and many others. Likewise,
there are a chorus of genuine nigger-boys ; five
trained racehorses, all winners at Rand wick ; an
intelligent mare which escapes from a blazing
stable with its mane and tail smouldering ; a big
explosion ; a horse-race ; and a lot of other sensa-
tions. Mr. George Darrell stage-manages, and
bets his immortal soul that “ Old Kentucky ” will
be a success.
* * * *
The Fitzgeralds’ show opened, as an-
nounced, at Sydney Exhibition Building, and
proceeded to live up to the high character which
The Bulletin gave it on its recent Melbourne
appearance. Mahomet, the Calculating Horse,
has probably read the notice of himself in this
paper, and he spreads himself on immense arith-
metical conundrums with the energy of an animal
who has a reputation to preserve. The horse
distinctly takes first place in popular estimation.
After him the public doesn’t quite know whether
it enthuses mostover the Roumanian beauty who
casts her plump and pleasing figure wildly from a
trapeze iu the topmost recesses of the building,
and comes down with 115 feet of back somersault
into the net, or the professor who does a similar
stupendous drop into the tank. The genuineness
of the professor is guaranteed by the water which
flies about 40 feet in every direction, but there are
signs that the Roumanian lady is likely to be the
favorite. The professor falls into the tank twice
—once tied up in a sack and once iu his ordinary
condition, and the Roumanian she-desperado
bounds from the roof twice—first forwards
and then backwards, so honors are even.
Perhaps if these two astounding persons
got up a competition and proceeded to drop
from the roof till further notice, the public
might be able after a while to decide which it likes
best. The Eclair brothers —particularly the big
brown brother who stands on his teeth on the end
of a stick while the green brother hovers around
and gives his moral support, do a quick, clever
performance; and Rose, the lady who skirt-dances
on top of a rolling globe, is abo above par and
nice-looking to boot. Altogether it is a bright
and novel show, and the management already
begins to call itself Fitz Gerald on the strength of
it.
* * *
Harry Plimmer now takes King Hedley’s
old billet at Her Majesty’s as the desperate rider
of the horse that falls into the stormy tank, and
Miss Lily Dampier assumes Miss Harrie Ireland’s
place as the wild girl of the plains in the deerskin
pants. Plimmer fills his new part quite as well
as his predecessor, but Miss Dampier is not by
any means an adequate successor for the hand-
some Harrie. She rides like a centaur, it is true,
and gallops across the biidge in excellent style,
but on foot, in the deerskin pants, she is some-
what disappointing. It is alleged that the ever-
welcome Alfred Dampier will shortly join the co.
at Her Majesty’s, and all Sydney will be pleased
to meet him once more.
* * » *
The old, dusty Gaiety awoke on Saturday
night and called itself the Bijou, and attempted
to produce a deeply solemn drama entitled
“Is He Guilty?” with the aid. of a number
of people whom this paper doesn't remember
hearing of before. Said drama, which struck
the audience in four sickening thuds, proved to
be a hash of the Dean case and the other case
of the corpse that was lately found in a box
at Woolloomooloo. The Gaiety is not a cheerful
house nowadays. The seats have been replaced
by long rows of hard, wooden chairs, which make
it look like a Stiggins meeting-house. The
enclosure for the orchestra has been removed, anti
a small, mortified piano squats nakedly on the
floor like a toad at the edge of a 640-acre paddock.
The big gas-jet outside refused to burn on Satur-
day night, and at last a pallid hand turned it off
at the meter. There was a thin smattering of
audience. The curtain didn’t go up till 8.30, and
m the interval of waiting the gallery yelled indig-
nantly, “Gimme back me sprat ! ” and urged the
co. to get its ’air cut. The orchestra proved to
consist of only one man, who came out, with a
scared expression, just as sounds of breakage
began to be heard from upstairs, and tinkled a
few notes, and the gallery told him to pull down
his vest, and someone rang an angry cow-bell
behind the scenes, and the curtain arose and
revealed a bad apothecary who held a mortgage on
the farm, and insisted on the daughter becoming
his ’appy bride before he would part with it, and
a noble bank-clerk who came in and paid off the
mortgage. Then The Bulletin man left, and
someone in the gallery dropped a hunk of hard
gloom on bis head as he made his escape.
* * * *
The Rickards show at the Tivoli is as
bright as ever, and that old-time favorite of
Sydney chappiedom, Ali.ss AIIcb Leamar, is on hor
way out from England to lend it some additional
glory. Most people- will remember Miss Leamar
as the most thoroughly enthusiastic high-kicker
who ever struck these shores, and the owner of
the wickedest-looking dark eye ever turned on an
Australian gallery.
* * * *
Professor Kennedy woke up his trance-
patient on Saturday night, and then moved on to
other spheres of usefulness. The savages of
Botany Bay have much enjoyed his show, and
never stopped to inquire into the miserable after-
fate of the wretches who consumed onions, kero-
sene, colza oil, cayenne, salt, candle-ends, and
other masses of beastliness for their degraded
gratification. It is alleged on what seems to be
good authority that these individuals fall into a
horrible state of indigestion behind the curtain as
soon as the hypnotic influence is removed, and
retch dismally lor an hour or two, and wish that
they were dead. According to this theory, the
man who devours kerosene and candles while’in a
hypnotic state becomes, when the influence is re-
moved, just like any other man who consumes
these same masses of grease, so anyone who
wants to understand the full inwardness of the
show can do it by getting 20 friends together and
drinking the Kennedy variety of slush along with
them, and then letting them all wail and collapse
together in the scullery.
• # «
Concerning another mesmerist who once
visited Australia, it is related that when he left a
certain city four of his subjects—all men of about
30—remained behind in the hospital. Someone
asked the doctor in charge what was the nature of
their complaint. “Nothing but extreme old
age,” he replied, “nervous wreckage and break-
up of the system. They’re fit for nothing now
save an Old Men’s Home.” These are some of
the consequences of the “funny ” variety of mes-
merism.
* * * *
The Gaiety co. has but a little time left
to live at Melb. Princess’s. A revival of “In
Town” crowds the house until this (Thursday)
evening, for public faith in Brad field is almost as
strong as before, and the fame of Fred Kaye’s
callboy has grown on reflection, so to speak, whilst
one last lingering gaze at Florence Lloyd in a
brown sac suit is a joy that mustn’t be missed.
Moreover, Melbourne’s former idol, Ryley, as the
Duke, appears to advantage in this show. His
performance of the harmless, elderly chappie sug-
gests that he will grow old gracefully, and The
Bulletin herewith wishes Ryley a full restoration
to robust health, and a happy white-headed dis-
tant future, in addition to a comforting middle
age. Also, this journal begs to inflict its heart-
felt blessing upon the adorable Miss Decima and
several other ladies of the co. who are, more or
less, about to get married, or have no intention to
risk it. The editor has hired an emotional bank-
clerk to go dow n to the steamer appointed to carry
the co. hence, and tear his golden hair when the
anchor’s weighed. After which he will commit
suicide by tatooing the names “ Hobson and
Palotta ” upon his heart. Saturday noon is the
time appointed for this tragedy. Meantime, Brad-
field will have a farewell matinee (25th) in oppo-
sition to the arrival of Brassey, and the abso-
lutely final appearance of everybody (in a mixed
entertainment) will happen when the sun goes
down upon the great vice-regal grovel.
* * * #
For Saturday evening next Messrs. Wil-
liamson and Musgrove purpose a grand produc-
tion of Brassey, K.C.B. — his first appearance as a
Governor in any Melbourne theatre. Lord
Brassey's sitting down is to be preceded by the
National Anthem and a brilliant display of
enthusiasm, and “The Old Guard” will bring
“ the most important Amusement Function of the
year ” to a suitable conclusion. Mayor Poly-
dore’s career is a sermon on the vanity of human
greatness when a crisis arrives.
* * # *
“ Joseph of Canaan,” which has brought a
good deal of sanctified grist to the mill, retires
from the Royal after Friday. The author is
understood to be coming to Melbourne during the
week, therefore certain reverend peacemakers
have formed a conspiracy for the reconciliation of
Messrs, Rignold and Walters. Any misunder-
standing between these two twin producers of
“Joseph” is regretable, now that their angry
newspaper conflict has exhausted its usefulness as
an advt. The hand of Providence seemed to
direct it for a time. But the harvest has been
gathered in, and the reapers ought to bury the
scythe. Let them think of how Joseph forgave
his brethren, and remember Pharaoh’s clemency
to his enemies. If they can be persuaded to kneel
side by side in the temple of Osiris, or something
of that sort, they will doubtless reconcile at one
fell swoop, and then, in the burning words of
Mrs, Potiphar, love shall remain. “Man to
Man,” a melodrama in many acts and sensations,
is due on Saturday.
* * # *
Harry Shine’s mellow cockney humor has
been added to the Rickards show at Melb. Opera
House, and that reliable establishment is still in
great demand, The little i.aiety Theatre re-
opened on Saturday with Larry Dooley and Frank
Clark at opposite corners, a tuneful variety pro-
gramme and the Calculating Boy. The infant in
question got his due meed of wonderment from
The Bulletin a while ago. His memory is still
a long way past all common understanding, and
the unexciting performance derives some interest
from the probability that one night he will lose his
duplicate brain and become like any other boy
who “cheeks” his father.
* * * *
“The Battle of Gettysburg” cyclorama,
formerly of Sydney, is now on view at the Mel-
bourne building, where “ Waterloo ” and “Jeru-
salem ” used to deceive the eye. “Gettysburg”
is not much the worse for wear. The Heavens
disclose a few creases, but otherwise it is quite as
convincing as in days of yore. Also, the lecture
retains its historical value, and the incident of the
gallant juvenile, who committed arson in free-
dom’s cause, and was subsequently slain whilst
plucking a flower from the roadside, comes in at
the old place as a warning to the young.

The last nights of “ A Gaiety Girl ” gave a
very poor idea of the same show in its early b'oom.
For some unexplained reason, Maud Hobson
dn>pped out of her place on the Monday, and a
new Lady Virginia Forrest was introduced by
Florence Lloyd. Miss Lloyd shaped very well,
but she didn’t go as far as the more gorgeous
charmer. Her chief recommendation for the
character, perhaps, lay in the fact that she toned
down the glaring difference between Bradfield’s
fussy Dr. Briefly and Monkhouse’s weighty im-
personation. Hence the assumption that Brad-
field (who cut a very small figure in his
canoodlings with Maud Hobson) had possibly
effected this alteration in the bill. Since his “ In
Town ” success, Bradfield has been—well, like all
other performers of his class, who wallow in
the greasy adulation of soft-headed newspaper
“ critics.”
* * * *
Bradfield, like Lonneu before him, de-
clines in the estimation of the stalls and circle as
he becomes still more popular with the youngest
and noisiest of the gods. He has been singing, at
Melb. Princess’s, a puny, witless, topical song, set
to the sickest of music. This sore affliction was
actually glorified in the advts., on the strength of
“five encores” from about the same number of
howling asses, who would have howled 10 encores
if their elders hadn’t called for silence. Bradtield
evidently believed in the song himself. And the
football-barrackers believe in Bradfield. He
realises their conception of “a fair treat.”

produces no vocalists worthy of the name. It isn't for
the want of good natural voices. Maybe the Sydney
dialect and our pronounctd nasal speech have something
to do with it. One well-known tenor (amateur) lat< ly
remarked to me: “There ain’t nobody in Sidney kin
learn me singhr. All yer've got ter do's to brealhe'from
yer stummick, watch yer time, ’nd prodooce yer notes
from the larnj x.” I suggested “ larynx." “ Well," he
rejoined, “you make it ‘laij’nx,’ and 1 make it ‘ lurnyx,’
’nd I'm goin’ ter stick ter larnyx, anywye. What could
Santley do with a man like that ?— Thorold W.

haw 1” announcements. They are both young Irish lads,
real name Lee. For “Cecil” read “ Walker” (no slang),

has seen Arthur Roberts. Roland Cunningham; of Melb.
Lyric Cluo renown, now playing with Roberts, writes out
that A.R. gags so much and so differently every night

When Cunningham made his first appearance Roberts
had just reformd from France, where he had met some
of tlie Melb. tenor s friends, and when he came on to the
stage he started telling him before the audience all the
funny stories lie had heard from the aforesaid friends.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback — the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

THE CUP
Will be the leading topic during the next few
weeks. Will the new Governor eclipse all his pre-
decessors by the magnificence of his equipage and
the number of his suite ? Will the costumes recall
the bygone days of Victorian luxury, the subsequent
reactionary modesty, or will they strike the “golden
mean ? ” Judging by the number of handsome cos-
tumes seen at Messrs. Manningand Co.’s, theBlock,
Collius-st., Melb., the modes of the present year
will rival all their forerunners by their beauty,
taste and richness, while the prices will demon-
strate how far general values have fallen since the
plenteous period of the boom. The dresses of the
more recent Cup meetings have not been of a very
“solid” character; in this respect the.present
year will mark a change ; and, should fine weather
prevail, the lawn on Cup-day will be radiant with
fair Women whose charms are enhanced by their
sartorial settings. Head-dresses will be unusually
bright this season, and Messrs. Manning and Co.
(Cullins-et., Melb.) are making a gorgeous display
of fashionable styles to tempt the intercolonial
visitors who throng Melbourne during the annual
racing carnival.
A PUZZLE.
Readers of The Bulletin in the country are often
puzzled to know where to send their orders, so
many drapery firms are advertising what they can
do, but few care much how orders are executed.
There seems to be a general idea that “anything
will do for the bush,” all forgetful of the fact that
ladies, even in the remotest backblocks, like to
have the latest styles and the freshest of goods.
Messrs. Riley Brothers, of 570 George-st., Sydney,
have for the past 30 years given special attention
to their Country Order Department, and know
intimately the requirements of every part of the
colony—North, South, or West. If the lady
readers of The Bulletin will look at this corner
of the paper every week they will see what we are
sending away every day. Gentlemen will find
articles for them in the corner of another page.
The largest stock of Nuns’ Veilings in the city,
in every shade of color, all double width, beautiful
fresh new goods for blouses or evening wear, 10id.,
12id. per yard.
Muslins for blouses and dresses. Shower of
Hail pattern, 6.3d. ; Tambour Spots and Stripes
in White, Cr am, Heliotrope, Pinks, Blues, all at
6Jd. per yard.
Large size Print Blouses, Is. 6d., Is. lid. ; and
some choice Muslin at 2s. Gd. each.
Riley Bros., 5G3 and 570 George-st., Sydney.
Extract from Dr. J. J. Miller’s lecture on
“Our Food Supplies, as reported in Melb. Age,
Oct. 7th : “Some 200 to 300 unborn calves went
into human consumption every week.”
The most emphatic literary success
achieved by any Australian in London during this
last 15 years was scored by Louis Becke with
“13y Reef and Palm” —his series of charming
South Sea stories reprinted from The Bulletin :
and now Mr. Arthur Barton Paterson (“ Banjo,’
of The Bulletin) has made, in his own country
and province, a record among Australian verse-
writers. Last Saturday morning, Messrs. Angus
and Robertson published about 1000 copies of
“The Man from Snowy River, and other verses ”
(chiefly from The Bulletin), which, to the very
last copy, were at once taken up by the Sydney
retail trade. No five-shilling book issued
south of the Equator has ever had such a recep-
tion. A second edition is now in the press, and
will be published on Monday.

Melbourne JEn Fete .
Melbourne folks look forward to their wonderful
Cup day as the greatest annual festival'in the
universe. Everybody, from the Governor to the
shoeblack, goes to Flemington that day, with the
exception of the burglar, who stays behind to reap
a harvest. The reason that everybody goes is that
admission to the flat is free ; if the customary
bob was demanded, many in that wonderful city
of smells would have to stay at home. Although
Melbourne abounds in paupers and blacklegs, she
also has her select and wealthy citizens, vyho
make the lawn and grand-stand on the First
Tuesday in November a perfect picture, and visi-
tors from the neighbouring colonies assist in the
events of the greatest day in. the Southern Hemi-
sphere. Visitors to Melbourne are advised to
take with them a supply of the famous B.L.
Corto Rothschild Cigars, which are the most deli-
cious smoke for (5d. the world has ever produced,
and no better cigar for the money will ever be
procured. The B.L. Cotto Rothschilds are
obtainable from all first-class tobacconists for 6d.*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
October 22, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Miss Aimis Montague's complimentary farewell
matinee, at Sydney Lyceum, last Monday, was
enough of a success to somewhat palliate the self-
advertising fuss of some of those concerned in its
promotion. Society was there in large numbers,
and the programme was the usual improbable
matinee mixture. “Pinafore” started it (with
Flo. Young as a melodious, sympathetic Jose-
phine), and the prison-scene from “ Trovatore,” in
which Sydney gazed for probably the last time
on Annis Montague’s shapely white arms and
hands —rang down the final curtain. In between,
the saddest item was, of course, the presentation
of an address and a necklace to Mrs. Turner—ex-
Mayor Manning spokesman. The visible misery
of these happiest moments in people’s lives makes
sensitive individuals glad when the ordeal is over.
Such things always remind me of the finish of a
hare-hunt —caught at last, and no getting away.
I must say that pretty, baby-faced Mrs. Turner,
whom Australia will ever remember as a true
artiste and a charming woman —by the pleased
simplicity of her manner, took a lot of the usual
rawness out of the thing. She was enthusiastically
bouqueted.
At the Montague matinee, by the way, occurred
that rara avis, a Sydney girl with a pretty voice
—one with very much the quality of Flo. Young's
flexible and sweet mezzo-soprano —added to which
she had a calm and self-possessed stage manner
quite devoid of the aggressive self-assurance
which so often marks otherwise meritorious
amateurs. Miss Hetty Holroyd’s item, well sung
and well “deported,’’ was a very pleasant thing,
and the public will want to hear more of her.
Maud FitzStubbs’s latest composition, “ Ave
Maria,’’ a study on a Czerny’s “ velocity,” has
been sung by Melba, who will take it with her
during her next American tour. With a sacred-
sounding vocal air, and violin and organ obligati,
the “ Ave Maria ” is a success on novel lines—
and certainly some pianoforte exercises are only
instruments of torture, because they are exercises.
If people took the trouble to fit song-music and
words to them they would, some of them, be
absolutely charming. Another song recently re-
ceived is “My Wish,” words by Lester Belling-
ham, the music by Oissie Naylor. Ihe best
thing one can say of this song is that words and
music are a beautiful fit. The halting mediocrity
of the one is faithfully mirrored in the other.
One day last week Madame Lucien Henry suc-
cessfully petitioned for a divorce from her hus-
band, the Judge being extremely sympathetic in
summing-up in her favor. After the case con-
cluded, Madame received the congratulations of
her many friends that at last the legal business
was over and a most unfortunate and painful
matter definitely arranged.
A brace of no-English-savee officers off one of
the French boats had an embarrassing experience
the other day in a fashionable Sydney drapery,
whither they went to purchase (in view of a Guv-
mentouse function) dress shirts with those bands
of insertion down the front so dear to the orna-
mental nature of the dapper Gaul, Arrived at
the rag-shop, they informed the obsequious shop-
walker that they wanted “ des chemises,” and
although French is taught for tuppence a week
in Australian State-schools, the “ Mr. Higgles ”
in question thought they required the usual
English article wiiich goes by that name—espe-
cially as they were keen about having “embroi-
dery,” and so sent them on with a flourish into
the ladies’-underclothing department, where a
blushing shop-girl awaited them.
" Some chemises ? Yes, sir!” and in a twink-
ling a box was on the counter full of fluffy, be-
ribboned, be-ruffied garments. “I am sure the
lady will like these,” said the shop-girl confiden-
tially, thinking she scented a marriage in the air.
But the blushing foreigners hastened to explain in
lightning pantomime that it was not what they
sought—they wanted “chemise pour ze night—
wiz embroidery vous savez,” and they drew
imaginary lines of insertion up and down their
manly chests to show the length of the fronts.
Then a new clue occurred to the girl, and she re-
turned with an armful of the most dainty, la.cy,
fairy-like night-dresses mortal woman could wish.
This last straw reduced Johnny Crapaud to
despair—the pantomime became violent, they tore
at their own collars, they shot their cuffs, they
waltzed with much by-play, to infer that a ball
was on the carpet and they wanted shirts tb wear
to it, while the sales-lady stood looking on wonder-
ing where they had escaped from. At this critical
juncture a Sydney woman who parley-voos and
who was shopping near by reckoned it would be a
Christian act to interfere and set things straight.
Talking about shirts, I hear that hand-painted
fronts are the latest caper for Johnniedom at
’Ome. They run in all patterns. 'One had a
trail of forget me-nots ; another was peppered with
pink roses; another had a flight of exquisitely-
painted butterflies, though their chielest charm
is their expensivenesa, “ for it all comes out in the
washing.” I don’t blame—well, I won’t say men,
but dudes—for trimming themselves thus. Even a
Johnnie can’t stand being dressed like his con-
temporary woman, and now that Eve has taken
his collar and tie, his cut-away coat, his cropped
hair and eke his breeks, Adam, in self-defence, if
he wishes to preserve Ins masculine individuality,
must cultivate a curly bang, a painted shirt and
crimson socks to prevent the sexes becoming
sartorially equal at least.
The wall-paper dickey hasn’t yet got to Aus-
tralia, though ’Ampden may bring one or two
with [lira. Sydney’s new and Gov. looks, on
paper, just the sort of man to have vicit us-look-
ing red roses climbing all over his chest when in
full fig. But, so far, the nearest approach to
fancy linen in Australia is the pink shirt with the
white collar, which always looks to me as if the
wearer was warming into life from the shirt up-
wards, like the Pygmalion story upside-down.
And —if 1 may still blushingly allude to the fashion
in shirts—l hear that they are now made like unto
a woman’s blouse, with most of the tails cut off,
which is a sensible plan, considering that men
don’t want width round the hips. The sexes will
never be equal in figure, for while a woman is in
despair if she isn’t shaped like an egg, a man
gives up all hopes of doing justice to his tailor
unless his figure somewhat resembles a capital T.
I have just discovered that the weird ghost
story which appeared in my letter of the week
before last was not original after all. The person
who told it to me as a perfectly new and hair-
raising experience either got it out of Sckibnkk’s
and remorselessly took in a lone scribe with
his second-hand yarn, or else the same awesome
incident has happened to two people, or possibly
more. Therefore, either the spectre or the man
was a plagiarist, and they can settle, the matter
between them. In future, however, lam going to
invent my own ghost stories, as the only method
of insuring absolute novelty combined with the
necessary blood-curdlingness, and any spook who
gibbers in this column hereafter will lie new, even
if he isn’t authentic. A spook doesn’t need to oo
authentic, anyhow.
N.S.W. Art Society is in luck, the late Mr.
Richard Wynne having left .£lOOO to provide
annually “the Wynne art-prize” for the Aus-
tralian artist producing the best landscape paint-
ing of Australian scenery. This will probably
mean a prize worth about .£33 per annum, and
will lead to the gum-trees getting a greater gruel-
ling than they have ever got before. Talking art
—the split in the N.S.W. camp continues to yawn,
and, as one consequence of it, the Art Society pro-
poses to part company with its capable teacher,
Mr. Julian Ashton.
The late Mr. John Hore, a border pioneer, who
died at St. Hilda the other day, left behind him
.£250,000 discreetly and justly apportioned.
Nobody who had any claim on the testator was
deliberately left out, and in case the grandchild
of a cousin fourteen times removed might have
been overlooked w hen the will was drawn up the
family residence and land at St. Hilda are to be
sold and the proceeds divided among relatives
who may have been forgotten. Ihere were no
charitable or religious bequests—the old pioneer
evidently believed in charity getting a fair start
at home. The only thing the church got out of
it was .£25 for the clergyman who read the burial
service.
Sydney must be populated by ghouls, w else
one wouldn’t read that “so much trouble is being
caused by the attendance of such large crowds to
see the man in the trance, that Professor Kennedy
made it known that to-morrow every person
wishing to ‘ view the body ’ ( ) will have to pay a
toll of sixpence.” Imagine the sort of vampire
who would pay sixpence to look at wh it is to all
intents and purposes a corpse! If such a
grisly show goes at sixpence a head, it strikes me
that a brilliant source of revenue is wasted by not
charging admission to the morgue, to hangings,
hospital-operations, accidents, and corpses in
general. Also, lectures on body-snatching with
gore-colored diagrams would probably make a hit.
To scientific people all horrors are open, of course,
but that the unscientific should enjoy gloating on
a living corpse, with no comprehension of the why
and wherefore, looks as if the evening papers had
at last accomplished their obvious task.
Fitzgerald Brothers’New London Company (the
circus is resting on a Maorjland farm) opened to
very cheerful business at Sydney Exhibition
Building on Saturday evening. The principal
feature of the huge audience was the absence of
bald-headed men, though the roseate vision of
Miss Adeline Antonio, with pale pink shapely
legs and a peg-top suit of pink-and-silver striped
satin as she appeared hanging from the daring
trapeze, ought to make bald-heads as common as
cobble-stones at the show. Miss Antonio must
have an iron nerve as well as hard muscles to drop
115 ft., and turn two back somersaults en route,
without breaking her neck or even relaxing her
pleasant smile. The other girl is La Belle Rose,
the swirl-danoer. Typically, I suppose, of the
grasp the graceful serpentine has over the whole-
world, La Belle Rose swirls her silvery draperies
on top of a globe. And she sent that sphere roll-
ing while she was making hay with her diaphon-
ous skirts, on which were thrown various colored
designs (I liked the pale-blue with silver stars
effect), culminating in political portraits. Of these
widower Parkes mingled best with the silvery
draperies—his long, white hair looked like flying
chiffon. And, apropos of portraits thrown on
dancers’ clothing—l wonder how that Tivoli dan-
seuse likes to remember how George Dean’s pic-
ture used to be flashed on her petticoats night
after night ?
La Belle Rose’s handsomest dress was a hand-
painted satin painted by herself on the voyage
out-butaltogether thenewLondono qmpanv make
a richly-dressed aggregate who wear different
clothes every niglit and won’t look at a pair or
baggy tights. The two Alton*, in black, scarlet,
and silver, suggested a couple of very spruce
birds as they hung on to the empyrean by one
eyelash, and Prof. Peart was a gloss of satin and
glimmer of pearls as lie dived from the roof. My
firm idea re the new London show is that most of
the members must be descended from the people
who supply the dark side of the plot in “ Paradise
Lost”—they fall from astonishing heights in such
a graceful and accustomed manner.
The greatest star of the “ Fitz ” show is, of
course, Mahomet, the educated horse. He is a
black, with two white hind-feet and a white star,
and is a beautifully-shaped and well-groomed
animal. His public performance is unique y
marvellous, and he is even cleverer in his stall v\ i i
nobody looking—which argues a lack of conceit on
the gee-gee’s part. 1 was permitted to call upon
Mahomet, the other morning, and was introduced
by Mr. Prohasco, his ’Murkan trainer. Mahomet
was observed to lie very cross about something,
and when asked if everything was correct
vehemently signified that it was not and no
until Mr. Prohasco removed his glossy silk hat
did the educated equine nod his head and look
amiable. Hats off indoors is Mahomet s idea or
good form. When asked how many ladies had
come to see him he knocked once with his hoot.
He not only answers the trainer’s questions, but
graciously replies to the most idiotic observations
of perfect strangers, and also adds up sums whic i
the persons who supply the figures probably
couldn’t compute as he does off-hand. At part-
ing he condescended to kiss me in the most
patronising way, and then the stable-door vvas
carefully shut and padlocked on the most interest-
ing horse it has ever been my good fortune to
meet.
Mr. SMITH, the North Shore chemist,
witness in the Dean case.
To-night (Thursday) the Royal Comic Opera
Co. fold up their grease-paint, roll their crape
hair and get things ship-shape prior to their
departure for Melbourne on Friday’s express
ready to open in Cupopplis on Saturday. “The
Mikado” was played on their last Saturday in
Sydney (fur the present) with Juliet Wray as
Yum Yum. By the way, when Miss W. played
the role in Brisbane and came to the dialogue
line, “ Yes, I am indeed beautiful,” a connoisseur
in Olympus scornfully ejaculated, “Kiddum!”
Flo. Young renewed her Katisha success. The
tuneful versatility of Florence may lead to her
starring as a double-voiced vocalist some day.
Josephine one evening, and Katisha the next—
soprano and contralto almostinone breath —argues
a most accommodating vocal range. Wallace
Brownlovv made the Mikado a cold-blooded, pierc-
ing potentate in black and gold, although an
adoring circle prefers its Brownlow not quite so
decayed in gait and make-up, and Vernon was, as
usual, grateful and comforting as Ko-Ko. What
a lovely tea-gown his dress would make!
The co. farewells to-night (Thursday), after a
good season, in acts from different operas just to
give the dresses and scenery an airing and shake
the moths out before packing up, I suppose ; for
certainly mixed bills are not the best
Audiences prefer their stage-plays “ straight ”
except, perhaps, when they are suffering a
matinee. The new operetta, which the co. is at
present getting into shape, is a very Frenchy
thing, yclept “ Cousin-Cousine,” wherein Latin,
I believe, spends two acts in a wild search after
his garments which he sheds in the perfectly inno-
cent but compromising manner peculiar to the
French. Brownlow is a dashing militaire, and
Flo Young plays the prima-donna part, which in-
cludes one rattling good song at least.
The renowned cyclist Zimmerman may not, in
Australia, prove to be the Zim. of old, the un-
married flyer who: beat three continents. The
cares of matrimony, I am told by an athlete lately
back from Adelaide, are taking the hum out of
Zim.’s wheel, and there need be no surprise if bis
star wanes in Australia, for lie promises to sprint
this trip on a buckled wheel —which comes of
taking two on a racing machine. The “ Daisy
B •!! ” theory is meant only for the honeymoon.
When Zim. got married liis wife said, “Arthur
is not going to race any more.” Which threatens
to be true, if read literally.
Passing through the lamp-lit corridors of Hyde
Park, Sydney, one’s attention is constantly
arrested by the attitudes of the cooing couples
with whom the park seats are ever profusely
adorned. It is interesting to notice that, in
nearly every case, the swain is the caressed —she
the oaresser. He, albeit spread generously in her
abundant embrace, takes his bliss complacently,
and appears to respond but diffidently to het
luxuriant fondling and general yearn, while the
nymph, as well as monopolising the emotion,
seems to supply the greater portion of fuel con-
versationally. Why is this? Are woman’s modest
mien and timid tongue a vain dream of daylight,
dispelled when the shades of dusk screen her from
the observation of captious critics, and is it coy
man that is decoyed, after all? I want to know.
I think the vessel which bears a large
theatrical company to fresh fields and pastures
new ought always to be called Ivoah’s Ark. There
is something so reminiscent of the animals which
went in two by two in the proceedings of Thespians
cn toute ; at least, if they don’t go in double-
banked they certainly come out that way.
Talking of the “ stige several recentlyrim-
ported and fatally-fascinating lie-actors do not
sport their natural wool but sail under wigs.
Such cute little wigs they are, too, that these
grease-paint Adonises have actually been getting
the adoration of women and the gnawing envy of
the men—especially those with a little bald spot
on top—for their “pretty hair,” as Lauri says.
But really a wig isn’t half a bad idea for foreign
mummers down “ under ” this season. Summer
is coming, and the Australian mosquito is.a fieice
and indefatigable animal, especially when he
smells lime-juice. These artificial Samsons will,
therefore, be well-advised if they glue, nail-tack,
or screw down their wigs to the very uttermost.
One of them, indeed, does keep his hair on very
desperately, but another occasionally takes off hie
ambrosial locks and waves ’em at people.
One entertainment offered to a certain frisky
theatrical co. will never be forgotten by either
guests or hosts. The latter, man-like, wished tc
make one happy family of the ladies with golden
hair a-hanging down their backs (in a manner of
speaking) and their own lady friends, quite a la
the Major’s garden-party in the “ Gaiety Qirl,”
so they boldly sent out cards —Lady Snooks to
meet Gold wash and vice versa. When the day
came behold the friskers turned out in full force,
but nary a society petticoat darkened the gate 1
Nothing occurred to save the situation, either,
except the enthusiastic manner in which every
man explained to every lady how his aunt or
grandmother (as the case might be) had beeji sud-
denly attacked with influenza or beri-beri only
that very morning. It was charming, I am told,
to hear how solemnly they tarradiddled to save
the cast-iron sensibilities of their gaudily-dressed
visi tresses.
My Melb. correspondent writes : —
“ So far as the animals were concerned, the
( recent parliamentary visit to Melb. Zoo was pro-
ductive of little pleasure. Presumably on the
strength of the animal-maintenance vote, many
members swaggered about the gardens with an air
of proprietorship, shying gravel and small pebbles
at the sleepy carnivora, teasing the monkeys, and
altogether frisking in a way which, if emulated by
a nobody, would precipitate a collision with the
officials. The lion, at no time a respecter of
persons, snoozed on persistently, despite the par-
liamentary tactics aforesaid, until the society’s
director twisted his tail and roused him to a
sense of his duty to the powers that be. The
tigers, in their turn, promptly lost their tempers
when poked up with a stick, and from then until
feeding-time the place resounded with the com-
plaints of badgered brutes, denied the poor privi-
lege of forgetting their hunger in sleep, and with
appetites whetted to ravening point by the
presence, almost within reach, of so many juicy
legislators.
“Last summer, winter, and the current season
all had their champions, sartorially speaking,
among the lady-visitors, who, as far as possible,
basked exclusively in the rays of their respective
hubbies, only inspecting the other Parliamentary
women from a distance like the rest of the ani-
mals. A massive military dame in sheeuy-greeny-
grey alpaca coat, skirt, parasol, and one of her
historic hats —the one with the lilac-tree, &e.—
was an impressive contrast to a diminutive poli-
tical person with tight curls all over her cropped
head and a lining of faded purple to her garment
of crushed grenadine ; while a florid damsel, in a
flame-colored silk blouse which attracted the sun’s
rays like a burning-glass, would have looked even
more like a pillar of fire than she did but for the
eooling proximity of a tall, fair-haired, dark-eyed
girl in cream crepou and a black sash.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The fact that Taverner, the Yic. Minister
who looks after the butter industry, is entrusted
with the Brassey reception arrangements is a fine
example of the fitness of things. The Brassey
reception is the largest butter problem Victoria
has yet met with.
• # *
Brassey’s arrival makes The Bulletin
feel quite sorry for Acting-Gov. Madden—the
man who has done his duty by his office by making
it do its duty by him. He has made more capital
out of his position than could have been believed
possible. And, what was stranger still, loyal Vic-
toria was beginning to kiss the hem of his trousers
as if he were himported Hinglish',' after all.
* * #
A touching sight on the first night of the
revival of “The Gaiety Girl,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, was the rival gubernatorial parties in
opposing stage boxes ; the Madden crowd on the
left, the Earl of Shaftesbury, Freeman Thomas,
Wallington, and ladies, on the right, and all
glasses on the latter box of tricks. It reminded
one touchingly of the domestic sorrows of the
family of John Ebenezer Firkins, the ex-Mayor
of London, as told by Hook in “Gilbert Gurney,”
* * m
Vic. Gov. Madden and suite spent two
solid hours the other day at Melbourne Treasury
awaiting the official visit of the Italian prince who
arrived per warship Christoforo Colombo. But
evidently a girl on the Block or some other
weighty attraction had crossed the princely path,
and, after swinging his legs for the time afore-
mentioned, the C. J. packed up his traps and went
home.

The Vic. Govt., in its exceeding worm-
someness, proposed giving Brassey four salutes of
17 guns, whereupon a proportionately solemn in-
dividual looked up the Imperial regulations
appertaining to the exploding of gigantic fire-
works and things when Governors move round,
and warned the Premier through the Argus that
Brassey is only entitled to two salutes. The
inference con veyed to Victoria’s trembling Govern-
ment is that something will happen to the
Empire and the local constitution will get kinks
in it; if more than 34 guns are fired by accident or
design on the landing of the new gubernator.

Miss CLAIRE LEIGHTON, the Gaiety girl who has married
a Sydney society youth.
“And,” adds the writer with inhuman gravity,
“ I am quite sure that Lord Brassey is much too
loyal a subject of the Queen and too good a sailor
and yachtsman to desire more.” The difficulty of
sticking to the etiquette of fireworks, and yet
giving Brassey all the explosions his soul may
yearn for is bound to lie heavily on Premier
Turner’s chest till the great function is over.
The Brassey reception arrangements pro-
vide for the multi - millionaire being stuck up
between St. Kilda and Melb. by four mayors in
succession. Lucky that he lands just as the
mayoral allowances for the year are granted. In
some other Australian municipalities the mayoral
chains would, even at this early date, have to be
taken “ out of soak” by public subscription before
their worships could appear “with ’em all on.”
• » •
A number of exiled Australians met
Brassey at Cape Town, and gravely assured him
that he was doing an honor to Victoria by accept-
ing the governorship of that province. They
might also have mentioned that he was doing a
great honor to the earth by being on it at all.
Meanwhile it is a solemn reflection that when
Brassey pegs out the honorable worm will do itself
the honor to eat him just like anybody else, and
probably the only remark it will make is that he is
beastly tough. After all, the worm is the only
genuine democrat.
* * *
The Brassey grovel will yet result in
murder being done in Melbourne. The latest is
that Gurr, M.L.A., accuses Premier Turner of
poisoning his Ex.’s mind—by cable, no doubt —
against Geelong, and preventing him landing at
that sad and sleepy town. There is no evidence
in the matter save that Brassey isn’t going to land
at Geelong, but Gurr assumes that a natural de-
sire to land at Geelong is implanted in the human
breast, and the fact that Brassey isn’t going to
land there is therefore sufficient evidence that his
mind has been poisoned. So Gurr demanded
in the House that Turner should cable to
Brassey, and assure him that all objections to his
landing at Geelong have been removed. But
Turner only sat and stared at Gurr with an un-
seeing eye, and, in consequence, Gurr will pro-
bably vote against the Government for the future.

be unable to lend his body as a show at 21s.
a head.

Day nurses in Melb.
Hospital have to work 72
hours a week including Sun-
days, while night-nurses go on
at 9 p.m. each evening and off
at seven each morning, thus
working 70 hours a week in
seven 10-hour stretches. And
such is “ sweating ” for sweet
charity’s sake.

There is a society of
ladies in Melbourne, the great
object of which is “to en-
courage a spirit of helpful-
ness, and a practice of kindly
speech,” and it calls itself
“ The Order of the Daughters
of the Court,” and announces
meetings of “Ye High Dame
and members of ye High
Court of ye Order of ye
Daughters of ye Court,” and
the members wear an insignia
that is a “real duck,” and
address each other in awful,
ancient gibberish, and carry
on in an idiotic manner gener-
ally. Which shows that the
painful, ape-like yearning of
the Mason, and the Oddfellow,
and the Druid, to wrap them-
selves round with gaudy rags
and tinkling symbols, and be
called Almighty Grand High
Bullyboy with the Glass-eye,
is not peculiar to man, but ex-
tends to woman also. There-
fore, she makes her deter-
mination not to call Mrs.
Brown over the way an old
cat, or her resolution to give
poverty-stricken widow Smith
a tract on the wickedness of
profligate living every day, an
excuse for calling herself a
High Dame, and going in for
the gaudy tomfoolery of an
Order. Good old advt. !
Emerson’s advice to the wise: “Hitch
your wagon to a star.” Melbourne grovellers’
precept: “ Hitch your punt to a ‘ Sunbeam.’ ”

The telegraphic-monopolistic combination
among Australian papers doesn’t promise a long
life. The two columns of special hogwash wired
from Albany by the Argus special about “Baby”
Brassey and “ Sybil ” (it was repeated to the S. M.
Herald) has made bad blood between Argus and
Age. Little occurrences of this sort, and the
awful sameness of the cable news, will lead to a
new rag with independent services ohipping-in
before long.
It now seems unlikely that the threatened new
Sydney daily will come out.

• * #
It would be well if the Australian clergy,
in their supplications for rain, would kindly
specify, for the benefit of the Almighty, the exact
amount of water they require. At present they
leave things so miserably indefinite that the
downpour varies from a Murrumbidgee shower,
four drops to the acre, to a ’9l deluge washing
half of the seaboard into the Pacific. If the par-
sons would but draw up a table of requirements
for various districts such as : “Bourke, one inch ;
Diamantina, 2|in., &c., &c., &c.,” and then pray,
“Send down, O Lord, rain according to above-
mentioned schedule! ” we should perhaps attain
something like satisfactory results. At present
they cure a drought by bringing a flood to the in-
dignation of two-thirds of the community.
* * *
The handsome giant, Placke, who came
to such grief in a prize-fight, has now been
a month in Sydney hospital, and his best day,
recently, found him still a little dazed and quite
inundated with lady visitors. Wien Placke’s
life was in the balance the leading surgical talent
of Sydney evinced the keenest interest in his case ;
now that he is getting better the women are taking
a hand. One nice girl came to the poor Dutchman
and read him a little sermon on the text of “ Birds
in their little nests agree” (Placke stands Gft.
sJin., and there wasn’t a bed in the hospital long
enough for him). Another girl called him “poor
dear,” and another “ poor boy.”
# * #
Hospital authorities, especially those “in
a large way,” should l>e somewhat cosmopolitan in
their attainments. The other day it was reported
of pugilist Placke that he awi ke from his stupor
and uttered a number of sounds, but that it could
not be discovered whether he was incoherently
raving or asking sensible questions in his own
language. Instances of such a kind are frequent
in small hospitals in remote localities, but they
should not occur in a seaport town the size of
Sydney.
# * *
“For the title ‘Agent-General,’” writes
an Anglo-Australian to Thk Bulletin, “ please
read ‘ Commission-Agent-Genera).’ An Agent-
General is a combination of head clerk, tourists’
guide, general factotum, petty ambassador, and
various other things. This department is a kind
of information-bureau, a colonists’ club, an
inquiry office, and a resort for the distressed.
Enrolment in its books is also a sure passport to
the distinguished society of the ex-Governors,
retired colonial politicians, pensioners, and per-
sons with various alphabetical appendices, whose
head-quarters are the Imperial and the Royal
Colonial Institutes. An obscure visitor from the
back-blocks, a J.P. from Swagville, a mayor from
Gum Town or member of the Shark Bay Harbor
Board, who aspires to bask for a space in the
society of pinchbeck aristocracy, and to return to
his colonial home covered with glory and a West
End rig out, must get the ‘open sesame’ from the
Agent-General.”
m m m
At the Prince of Wales’s levees uniform or
Court-dress is de rigueur. It is usual, however,
for Australians in London, who seek after the
bubble reputation, to hire a Court-dress at a cost
of five guineas from a certain swell Regent-street
pawn-shop. The full rig-out costs about .£3O, in-
oludingeoat, vest, knee-breeches, &c., but wealthy
colonists, who want to do the thing “ regardless,”
pay a big price for diamond buttons.

A “ kommers’" night at Melb.
Turn-Verein is an experience. There is
good music, beer and—more beer. Per-
fervid Deutschers, with astonishing hair,
spring on the tables, and orate from op-
posite ends of the hall on the guest of
the evening. When the torrent of gut-
turals has ceased all glasses are filled,
waved about with diabolically mysterious
gestures, and then half-emptied to the
terrific cry of wassail. Then follows a
chorus and the glasses are emptied in one
simultaneous gulp and held up for proof ;
then brought down with a thundering
clatter on the tables, which concludes the
ceremony. The night given to Kruse
commenced on Thursday, and some kept
it up till mid-day on Saturday. A pro-
minent member explained how it was
done. It appears that whenever a Turn-
Vereiner feels the lager overpowering he
does not go home and beat his wife like
an Englishman, or throw stones at a
Chinaman like an Australian—he simply
retires into the adjoining gymnasium.
There half-an-hour on the parallel-bars
and trapeze sobers him up, and he comes
back well strung for more.

Editor Bulletin, —Be the “Social Can-
cer.” You place all the blame as regards abor-
tion on the attitude of society towards the erring
spinster : she must, to avoid social disgrace, rid
herself of her incubus. No doubt true, as far as
it goes, but my experience is that married women
are far and away the worst offenders. Here the
question of social disgrace cannot enter, except in
cases too rare for computation. I have had ex-
ceptional opportunities for coming to a conclusion,
and were the abortionists to depend on spinsters
they would not exist financially for a month. A
most serious matter for the community is the way
that women are shirking their duties and functions
as mothers. The women who peopled Aus-
tralia in the fifties and sixties had their families
of five or six as an average, and were all the better
women for it. Women’s function is to bear
children ; hence the proper exercise of that func-
tion tends to mental, moral and physical develop-
ment. The abrogation or suppression of that
function must result in individual and eventually
in general degeneration. (Note the increase of

The New Women respectively of the Biddy
and the Giddy school don’t know what they want.
The Biddy school want a blundering lot of good-
hearted old women to take charge of tragic
motherhood, and Biddy is only Mrs. Grundy in
balloon sleeves. The Giddy school want some-
■ how to drink deep of the cup of life and avoid the
dregs of motherhood. Biddy is good-hearted,
but her tongue is too long. Giddy is bright, but
has no sense. Biddy has some idea in her head
that motherhood is an enforced affair and should
be abolished as such. Giddy wants to abolish
fatherhood.
• • •
Mackay farmers are growling at the
inferior quality of the Kanakas lately introduced.
One recently arrived with a contracted hand,
another with an incurable, chest trouble, a third
with half a foot, &c. Mackay shouldn’t grumble ;
most of the strong-healthy islanders are at present
manuring with their bones plantations on the
Burnett and Pioneer, and consequently sugar-
towns have only the halt, maimed and blind to
fall back upon. Tommy Tanna, by the way, is
waxing marvellously cute. A Polynesian came
to Mackay the other day with an abscess in his
back, and proved incurable and consequently use-
less. In a burst of confidence he informed his
hapless employer : “ Me savee that fellow lump
no good ; me go longa Queensland plantation look
out doctor.”
More cold-blooded secret murders by imported
niggers in the North. The vigilance-bell will be
ringing shortly at Port Mackay,

Some N.S.W. railway officials got a severe
shock last week. A distinguished English visitor,
not many days resident in the province, was ten-
dered the customary free-pass but refused it, and
enquired the price of a monthly tourist-ticket,
which he expressed his intention of buying !

“ Only a few weeks ago an English lady,
acting as nurse in Melb. Hospital, died of
disease contracted through having a scratched
finger whilst swashing a patient. Last week
another lady-nurse died in same hospital from
blood-poisoning, also through a scratched finger.
Considering the risks run the pay (usually .£5O a
year) is miserably inadequate, yet “ respectable ”
employment for well-educated girls is so scarce in
Victoria that for every vacancy on the hospital
nursing staffs there are 200 or 300 applicants.
* # »
The sweating of hospital nurses. Melb.
hospital, 72 hours per week; Castlemaine hospi-
tal, 80 hours per week, one afternoon a week and
every alternate Sunday off; Melb. women’s hos-
pital, 91 hours and 82 hours in alternate weeks,
with (in the long week) 16 hours on duty on
Wednesdays and Saturdays; must pay entrance
fee of £0 55., find own uniform, and give one
year’s services for nothing; time allowed for meals
about 20 minutes each.
» * *
N.S.W. Aborigines’ Protection Board has
decided to appeal, if necessary, to the Supreme
Court for a definition of what legally constitutes
an aboriginal—the lower courts having determined
that under the Licensing Act a half-caste is not an
aboriginal. There are several things which an
aboriginal may legally do and which others may
not do, and vice versa ; and as half-castes arc
aboriginals under certain Acts and pronounced
not to be aboriginals under others, they are likely,
failing a definite settlement of the matter, to be
“ made use of ” in many fashions.

Melbourne has a 16 - stone bloomered
cyclist. Which recalls the fact that in a Yankee
town some anti-bloomerists hired a 2001 b. negress,
dressed her in rainbow-striped bloomers, sent her
pedalling round town—and killed the bloomer
party in one act.

A gentleman who runs a massage business
on a first floor in a Northern city got a
bad shock the other day. Whilst engaged in
treating a buxom young married lady, the
patient’s husband, who had, with difficulty, traced
his wife, demanded admission so fiercely that the
masseur dared not open the door. The husband
loudly invoked the aid of the tenants in the
building to I >• ik the door open ; then the masseur
barricaded t lace, believing hubby to be mad on
hearing him offer “ .£lO to get at the man inside ! ”
At last the man of science lowered himself out of
the window into the street, and when the angry hus-
band did get in he found his spouse in tears-and
that was about all. Hubby has now rented an
office close to the masseur, and sits outside his
office all day and sleeps on the masseur’s doormat
all night—the alleged reason for this being that
his wife forget to pay the masseur for treating
her, and he wants to settle up. But, up to date,
that masseur has not returned.

Bicycle stables are supplying a new felt
want. Instead of riding your trusty steed right
along to the office, and leaving it in a draughty
passage all day to catch cold or get “ shook ” by
thieves, you dismount at some adjacent stables.
There the bike is fed with oil and' carefully
groomed in readiness for your return journey.
The charge for stabling and bait is 3d. per day, or
Is. a week, and the idea presents a picturesque
element. Golden-haired girl dashes into stable-
yard, leaps from saddle; ostler, with straw in his
mouth, touches his cap, leads bike into stall, and
proceeds to clean it up, hissing the while as though
he were putting a polish on a norse.
Contrast. One Blundell threw three
gallons of boiling water over his wife, nearly
scalding her to death. Fiued <£2o or six months.
George Patterson struck a policeman over the
head. Sentence, 10 years and a dose of the lash.
One scalded wife, six months with the option.
One bruised policeman, 10 years and the lash.
Moral plain.

Dr. Hodgson, who went under in the
Phillip (Sydney) election last week, is the author
of a proposal which found official favor a few
years ago. It was to the effect that when a
suspicion of foul play rested on anyone the
autopsy on the body was to be performed by a
State official together with a medical practitioner
appointed by the suspected person. Were con-
stituted authority always as anxious to clear away
suspicion as it is to crystallise suspicion into cer-
tainty the proposal would have been adopted long
ago.

The sweets of arbi-
tration. Lodge patient
comes to lodge chemist
with order from lodge
doctor for 6 gross, i.e.,
eight hundred and sixty
four pills. Chemist gives
him 36 doz., i.e., four
hundred and thirty-two
on account, and says
he can have the rest
when lie has taken that
lot. Man wants all at
once, and reports chem-
ist to lodge. Ruling—
chemist should have sup-
plied the lot at once, as
the man lived in the
bush. Fact that man
had doses enough for
months scornfully
ignored. Fact of three
mails a week to patient’s
place of residence more
scornfully ignored. Fact
that chemist had not
refused to supply all,
but only wished to
supply in two batches
for convenience’ sake,
still more scornfully
ignored. Lastly, fact
that patient had died
after taking only 3doz.,
so that there were still
33 doz. available for
burial with him, most
scornfully ignored.
At a recent M.L. weddipg the bride hesi-
tated over the word “obey,” at which the groom

At Bendigo, last week, a young woman who
had cut her throat because she was falsely accused
of theft was brought up for sentence, and Judge
Holroyd decided that, according to law, he must
order her to be executed. After looking up pre-
cedents, however, he decided that, though she had
to be nominally executed, he had power to let the
prisoner out on the probation system, so she was
released on giving her own recognisances to come
up and be hanged when called upon. If she
doesn’t come up and be hanged when called upon
her recognisances will be forfeited, which will be
a truly dreadful thing, and the prospect of it is
calculated to make any girl turn up in good time
for execution. Altogether, this particular prisoner
lias had a depressing time. She was falsely charged
with theft in the first place, and then after having
a large gash in her throat, and undergoing a slow
process of repair, and spending some time in
prison, she is now awaiting execution on her own
recognisances. Presumably she will continue in
that state for the rest of her life, and the law
probably calculates that the knowledge that she
is waiting to be hanged on her own recognisances
will make her walk a chalk-line for ever after-
wards.
* * *
An Italian organ-grinder in Sydney has got
four months’ gaol for habitually biting his small
brother when the latter did not collect enough
money. A medical examination of the small
brother revealed the fact that he had been bitten
all over, and the practice had evidently been or
long standing, as many of the scars appeared to
be the remains of very old bites. It is alleged
that music is a soothing influence, but either there
are exceptions to this rule, or else the hand-organ
isn’t music, or else the Italian was really soothed,
and it was the music that prevented him biting
his little brother a lot more than he did.

ODE TO A N'ERALD BOY.
Shine out, O smudgy kid !
Let not your light be hid :
’Twas meant to gladden
The eyes of men who need
A hevenin’ rag to read—
Including Madden.
When customers draw nigh,
How dulcet is your cry,
How proud your bearing.
One never sees you fight
With rival boys at night,
Or hears you swearing.
You never force the sale
Of “ pypers ” with a tale
A t all untruthful :
You ne’er, in dullest times,
Imagine gory crimes
With fancy youthful.
Your language, soft and sweet,
Makes music down the street;
’Tis strictly proper.
We don’t believe as how
You’d stoop to stone a Chow,
Or “cheek” a “copper.”
Shine out, dear urchin small,
Example bright for all
The base and bad ’uns !
Diffuse your virtue rare;
This is our heartfelt prayer—
And also Madden’s.

The Wild-Cat Column .
One proviso in tlie S. A. State Bank Bill is to
the effect that the Slate Bank shall be the only note-
issuing institution in South Australia, and that its issue
is limited to £600,000. except in times of special stress,
when It may be increased by proclamation to £750,000.
The notes are to he redeemable in gold on demand at the
head office of the bank. Under these circumstances the
restriction to £600,000 seems utterly stupid and unneces-
sary. So long as nobs are redeemable on demand the
public will only take as many of them as it wants for
trade purposes, and it should get as many as it wants,
whatever that number may b°. It is impossible to unduly
inflate the currency while the StSte Bank pays gold for
all notes presented to it ; under these conditions-the cur-
rency regulates itself. The proposed limit is about £1
18s. of paper money per inhabitant, and in France the
circulation is more than twice that amount, while in the
U.S. it equals £3 per head. The S.A. State B ink is to
hold a gold reserve equal to one-fifth its circulation, the
other four-fifths being invested In Government bonds and
other securities. Under these conditions the institution
will be equally secure whether its note-issue is £lOO,OOO
or ten millions, and the amount should be unlimited,
its regulation being left to popular demand.

The N S.W. Bank Notes Act of ’93 is on tlie
point of expiring, and as nothing lias b a en done to
replace it by a permanent m -asure, the old state,of chaos
will presently supervene. The worst features of all are
that, on its expiration, not-s will cease to he a first charge
upon assets, and anv wildcat institution in N.S.W.
which chooses to call itself a bank, may issue notes at its
own sweet will. Recently, a good business lias been done
in other provinces with the rag monej’ of tlie Provincial
and Suburban and other long-d f 11 net institutions, and
even the pap-f of the ancient and forgotten Bank of
Queensland has been found in circulation. The average
man way back doesn’t know one bank from another, and
is prepared, except in times of panic, to take anything in
the shape of a bank-note which anyone may offer him,
and when he can he fooled w ith the paper of an institu-
tion which died 20 or 30 years ago, he is capable of taking
the alleged securities of an)’ “bank” living or dead.
Tlie pr -sent’A ‘t lias done a great deal to prevent the
issue of shoddy currency in N.S.W., and its expiration
Anil be a public calamity.

Th ' N.S.W. L°g. Council lias hurried through
a Bill to permanently establish the reconstruction prin-
ciple whereby the busted company rises from the
financial dead, and postpones its liabilities for ten years
or so, and goes on ag tin as if nothing had gone wrong.
What will happen to it in the Assembly is still doubtful,
but the Labor members, at all events, will probably give
a solid vote against it. Still, there are many good points
about the reconstruction idea, if it is based on equitable
principles ; the mischief so far is that it hasn’t been based
on anything resembling equity. The first essential pro-
viso is that, before a co. gets permission to reconstruct,
there should lie a searching investigation into its affairs
- hy State-appointed officials, and if the State auditors re-
port that, it is capable of fulfilling the proposed arrange-
ment, whatever that may be, and that arrangement is
assented to by a majority of the creditors, then it should
lie allowed to reconstruct as much as it wants to. Tlie
second prm iso is that the creditors should thereafter be
represe Red 01 the Board until their claims are paid in
fill . Uud r these conditions even the much-abused re-
constnicti- n device is vas'ly preferable to promiscuous
liquidation. Liquid ition is a process by which tlie assets
disappear faster than a snowball in hell, and no one
knows where they go to, save that the lawyers get a lot
of them, for app aring in court and asking permission, at
a cost of .t’2.) or so in f -es, to accept 30s. from shareholder
Smith in It u of the £7 10s. he owes, on the ground that
theoiher £6 as irrecoverable. Also, though much lias
b en said about the slow agony of reconstruction, the
agmvof liquidation is generally a lot slower. Nothing
o 1 llrs ea-rtn is slower than a big, fat, pompous liquida-
te ■.

The City of Melbourne Building Society
is still liquidating. It has properties of the nominal
value of £276,000, and other investments, and at present
the total income from all these is about £4OOO annually.
Repairs, taxes, costs of liquidation, &c., come to about
£9OOO a year. Cons quently it seems that it costs £5OOO
a year to own about £300,000 worth of alleged assets.
Only a very rich man could afford to be a ''millionaire
on these terms.

matter. T ueir bits of land —fur which some of
them pail as mu ah as £4OO in cash—in the

Tlie Royal Bink of Australia has closed the
ha'f year with a net profit Of £2039-a slight falling off
from the £2130 earned last, half-year and the £2874 fur
the ha'f year end ,-d 30th S 'pt.,’94. Compared with the
figures of a year ago th ■ note circulation has decli led
goni ’what; th 1 itnn, “ bills in circulation,” has dropped
nearly one-half; a id the d posi s hive dim’nished fnm
£255,000 to *.216,000. A mong the assets, advinccs have
dec i icd by £13,000, hut though the total liabilities have
been red tic d by £3B,O'K) the cadi res'rve has i icreased.
Th ■ I! >yal has now £439,826 in cash against liabilities to
the p iblic of onli £2:3,000, equal to not less than 37 per
cent. There is’ag.abi no div. and the balance to credit of
profit and |c#>fdj-incli now ammnts to £17,378, is carried
forward. T ie U iyal frankly admits tint it will be necessary
to write off n portion of its capital as lost when the long-
promised Companies Act is passed so as to allow of such
an operation ; for the Vic. law, astonishing ns it may seem,
doesn’t at present permit the writing-off of lost capital.
But though the directors have, in their last three reports,
mentioned that it will he necessiry to write off capital,
they' have not once explained the amount of the bank’s
losses, though the fact that the net profits only equal
about £1 Bs. per cent, per annum on the nominal paid-
up capital is, evidence that a very considerable propor-

nominal one, at present. The balance-sheet lumps
■together bills receivable in Victoria, landed property,
Govt, stocks, loans on mortgage, and all manner of
securities in one big item, after the usual obscure bank-
ing fashion. The best featu-csof the document are the
reduction in expenses, the big cash reserve, and the f ict
that the bank fittings, furniture, stationery, &c. f are put
down at the very moderate figure of £3BO. As the Royal
lias certain losses to meet it might as well begin to pro-
vide for them by writing off the £17,378 at credit of profit
and loss, which, under existing conditions, is a mere
ornament. Also, it might inform its shareholders how
they really stand.
• • * »
If tire Victorian Companies Bill passes in its
present shape it will make a revoluti in in the local bank
balance-sheet. According to its provisions that hitherto
murky document fnust show separately among the assets
how much is held in Govt, and municipal securities, with
particulars of the securities, iiow much in lulls and pro.
notes, in cash on hand or in banks, in real estate, loans
on mortgage, loans on other securities (nature of securi-
ties to be specified), loans without securities, and shares
of other companies (the names of the companies to be
specified). The securities in which the reserve fund is
invested must he s-t forth. Also, among the liabilities
must he fully explained the amount due on deposits bear-
ing interest, deposits not bearing interest, mortgages,
bank overdrafts, judgments, bills and pro. notes,
and contracts. Some balance - sheets, under these
new conditions, will be very interesting reading.
Still one or two important factors are omitted.
Tlie balance-sheet ought also to state whether the
assets are taken at their original cost, or, if they are
taken at a valuation, when that valuation was made.
When the usual statem-mt is advanced that allowances
have been made for had and doubtful debts, it should
also be clearly set forth bow much allowance has been
made. Under present conditions the allowance may be
lid. Farther, while the bill makes directors criminally
responsible for false statements, it should extend an equal
responsibility to managers and auditors. And, above all,
it Should provide for a State audit. The auditors, at pre-
sent, are appointed by the shareholders. The directors,
by virtue of their own shares and their proxies, can
generally command the biggest vote at the shareholders’
meeting, so they very often appoint the auditors. And
naturally, if things are crooked, they appoint men who
won’t expose them, or ask awkward questions, or look into
tilings that are not meant to be looked-into.
When the Vic. Parliament is on the job of com-
pany-reform, it should also provide firs State audit of
life-assurance sooieti s, and should enact that their
balance-sheets shall show the following things; (1) The
ameunt received as rent, interest on mortgages, and in-
terest on other investments, separately; (2) the amount
under each heading that is accrued but not due, and the
amount in arrear. And it should bo made a felony of the
deepest dye to disguise interest arrears by lumping them
in with th“ capital of the mortgages, by which device in-
terest maybe charged oo hopelessly-dead securities for
years and years, and taken credit for as profits, and dis-
tributed among policy-holders, without anything appearing
in the balance-sheet to suggest that both’the interest and
the principal are dead and gone and utterly lost. Some
societies don’t even profess to show arrears. Others do,
but there is no possible guarantee that they show all the
arrears, and The Bulletin strongly suspects that in some
cases th°y only show a small amount as a guarmtee of
good f lith, and lump the rest with the mortgages. Tiiis
is the weak spot in insurance business. The only way the
public can f irm anything like a reliable estimate of the
safety of the investments is by noting the amount of
interest which is actually collected each year, and the
amount which is due hut doesn’t come in. And the
societies don’t show this in any trustworthy shape.
Sometimes the public has the management’s own per-
sonal assurance on the subject, which doesn't necessarily
amount to much, and somniruos it hasn’t even that,
which amounts to a good deal less.

J.T. (Kurnalpi) : Groat ignorance prevails as
fo what a Mint or can do, for the miner. You can
yourself smd parcels of gold registered post to Melb.
Mint for purchase at Mint value. The bank now sends
your po'd there, and you could save the middleman
profits by doing the business yourself. The cost of pos-
tage would be about 4Jd. per oz. —21 oz. make an
economical small parcel to send. Toe Mint will remD. the
money to you in whatever way you wish, or your friends
can collect fir you. If you suspect the price your gold-
buyer gives you, there would be no harm in sending two
or three ounces to Melb. (or Sydney) Mint, fir purchase ;
you will then get a document showing the actual assay
and value of your gold. Now that the W.A. Govt, has
decided on a local Mint it shoul I be pushed ahead for the
sake of the industry. Bankers and storekeepers are
get ing more than their fair profits on gold-buying.

for three failures. Everything has been rising. The
really useful mining tipster is the fellow who can tell you
when the rise is going to stop and the boom to burst.

Despite the recent N.S.W. legal decision that
traffic in unused railway or steamer tickets is illegal, the
business of the licket-scalp"r flourishes. There are two
recognised men in Sydney at the game, who keep decent
offices and clerical staffs, and are making money. They
have correspondents in the other provinces and European
centres, and if a ticket cannot be disposed of in one place
it is sent to another. Their ramifications extend to Eng-
land and America, besides I he interprovincial steamer
and railway routes. £2OO each per week is no uncommon
turnover.
» * * *
The notorious Colo Valley water-power grab
scheme is once more being galvanised. The idea has
always hid plenty of money behind it, but its backers
were chary of parting with much till the Enabling Bill
had become law, which may probably account for its not
being introduced during last N S. W. Parliament. Amongst
others connected with the venture this time are a pro-
minent and much-travelled politician, and a very high
and influential Stale official.
* * ♦ ♦
The reoent rains have proved a godsend to the
present proprietors of the Hillgrove and Armidale
Electric Power concern. Formerly there was but suffi-
cient water to keep going the lighting plant, which barely
paid expenses. Now the Eleanors, Baker’s Creek, and
Golden Gate Co’s are in full swing, and use both power
and light—a big thing for the Electric people.

The Anchor tin-mine (Tas.) held by many to be
a more or less uncertain venture at its pr sent capital,
has the name of Premier Braddon on its prospectus.
Opposition leader Dobson lately moved in the Assembly
that no Minister should lend his name and official designa-
tion to a mining or other speculative venture. The legis-
lators didn’t desire to talk about the matter, but flattened
out the resolution by a silent two to one majority, and
hurriedly changed the subject. The Anchor, it would
appear, was sold for £6OOO, re-sold in London for £20,000,
and re-floated for £150,000.
