Dear Bulletin,—Whilst N.S.W. Mining on
Private Lands Act is being put through, some information
ought to be given to members of Assembly, who do not
understand mining, re the muddle that the ’9l Act
brought in. It is a positive trap to gull the public and
satisfy the land-holders. On one district field
the royalty demanded, and paid, is 25 per cent,
gross gold ! Of course, only the plum claims can pay it;
but there are many lOdwt. to 15dwt. and up to loz. reefs
that would be worked as ordinary block oliims. I believe
1 am right in stating that not one lease has been granted
on application (except under the private-agreement
clause) under the new Act, This, of course, is simply
holding back the land until the eyes are picked out. The
miners here openly confess that their only chance of
getting anything through quickly is per the member for
the Bogan Bridge at Monkey. I believe that for the same
reason .leases under the old Act have been delayed and
shunted so that the applicants may conceal the auriferous
deponts and hold them in reserve. I have reported part
of what I have seen to the Minister for Mines, and have
pointed out to Mr. Siee the glaring absurdity of the
alleged mining here. It is notable that there are no
visible union men here. The aid from the p ospecting
vote has been granted per backstairs. One of the great
multi non-working claim-holders has just told me that
his name was put down as requiring Government aid by
a certain M.P., and that without his sanction. If that is
not roads-bridges-cum-vote-flshing, then I have no
morals.—Phil Mocbrat.
♦ • * *
Certain big Melb. drapers have been getting
their trade-circulars printed in England and thence
posted in open envelopes to their clients in Victoria—thus
depriving the Vic. P.O. of revenue and the local printers
of work. The authorities have now got level with this
system by collecting duty on the circulars as “imported
printing.”

Some more about the Ernest Giles Prospecting
Co. 600 shares are to be issued to test and develops the
finds made by the prospector; and it is proposed to
authorise the directors to dispose of any claims dis-
covered. This latter is extremely desirable, The Bul-
letin belief being that of the two mining asses—the man
who buys a mine and the man who won’t sell one—the
latter is the worse.

The Young O’Brien mine, Grenfell, N.S.W.,
has been placed under offer, and is going to London.
Mr. Woodhouse, one of the many smilers on Ciolgardie,
lias lately reported. The mins is a fair one: reef from
2 to 4ft., run of gold nearly 203 ft. long, but largely con-
fined to a mineralised streak in the reef, which has’so far
averaged about an ounce to the ton. The Young O’Brien
should pay on a small capital, but is not good enough to
stand a big one.

The prospectus of the South Mount Lyell Ex-
tended Mining Co; (Tasmania) appears in the advertising
columns of this issue. The lode in this mine, it appears, con-
sists of a large pyrites formation, averaging from 2ft. to
12ft. wide. Bulk assays return loz. 12dwts. gold, 12oz.
silver, and 17 per cent, copper. Two trenches have been
cut, one 15ft. long by 4ft. wide, and one 12ft. by 3ft., and so
far as the investigations have gone the lode is solid and
with every prospect of permanence. Only 1200 shares were
offered to the public, and they have already been rushed,
therefore the prospectus is chiefly offered for public
information.

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at tke
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added bo
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.
Dangers of Travel.
The dangers incidental to travelling seem to he
increasing rather than diminishing. Almost daily
it is our painful duty to record appalling deaths,
on land and sea, and it would almost seem as il
the progress we are supposed to have made in
scientific appliances, in place of minimising, en-
hances the chances of danger. But on more care-
ful consideration we find it is not so much that
science is at fault as that men are careless.
Carelessness is the actual cause of a great per-
centage of the most terrible catastrophes and
deaths, and as responsible persons ignore
this fact the present death-rate will continue. To
permit the kidneys to rot away, or to suffer lime-
stone deposits to accumulate in the bladder, is
criminal carelessness, especially when a sure and
safe cure is obtainable. Mr. Thomas J. R.
Bakhap, Mine Proprietor, Hobart, Tasmania,
says : “lam pleased to say that some years ago

chews cigars—sometimes all day long. And he

Dr. L. L. Smith’s (Melb.) telephone num-
ber is 666, which, according to the prophets of the

Chief Secretary Gordon (S.A.) has a
daughter engaged to be married to “ Jim ” Ander-
son, a rising lawyer with Parliamentary am-
bitions.
Chief Justice Way has acted 19 times as
Governor of S.A. The last period has been for
nine months, and there were two six-month terms
previously.
The father of Sydney aldermen is William
Kippax, who has represented Cook Ward fora
third of a century without a break. He has never
aspired to the mayoralty.
Lieut.-Gov. Way, who has been carrying
out the vice-regal business in Adelaide “ at re-
duced rates to meet the times,” says he loses iIOO
a month on the salaries of his staff.
It is said that Kingston, Q.C., and Symon,
0 G., have healed their recent feud. Both were
articled clerks with Chief Justice Way, and
Symon’s firm succeeded to the business.
A few evenings since, Speaker Berry
(Yic.), tired with a long sitting, had a gjass of ale
brought in by an attendant, and placed on the
stool by the side of his chair. A unique incident.
If Windeyer and Way could find the man
who told the story of the tears and prayers at the
Fitchett farewell in Adelaide recently, he would,
if legally possible, be hanged without benefit of
clergy. .

of a thousand temples.” She will not visit Aus-
tralia this year, bub will leave the local Theosophists
to work out their Karma by themselves, now that
“the Countess” will be joining the rest of the
cult in India. Theosophy in Australia has not
been heard of so much since the much-revealing
Judge split.

* Yeer an iron-

after Salmon Wilson, was the late Francis Ormond,
who gave it .£20,000 to found M-arshall Hall s
chair of music. He also gave to the affiliated
Ormond College during his life £41,780, and left
it by will over £66,000 His contributions to uni-
versity education alone thus come to close on
£ 130,000.

Dear Bulletin, —An
example of how Freetrade
works out in England. I
met a Chicago wool-buyer
on a Cunard steamer who
had bought, on behalf of
several American firms,
upwards of £200,000 worth
of Australian greasy wool
at London sales. This
wool was to be taken to
Chicago, worked up with
a mixture of cotton, sent
back in the form of piece
goods to London, made
up into clothing by sweat-
ers, and exported to Aus-
tralia. “ Comment is
needles.” —M.

J. J. Walsh, barrister, died at Drouin,
Gippsland, Oct. 15. His wife was Fawkner’s
widow, and he named his place Walsh vale, after
John Pascoe' Fawkner’s Pascoevale. Walsh was
of slender ability, and a ridiculously nervous bar-
rister. Nevertheless, he rendered great services
to Victorian Liberalism, in its earlier stages.
H. : “Pastor Walters was unfairly
jockeyed out of the Melb. Unitarian Church. He
found the congregation worshipping in a miserable
little* white-plaster square tomb of a building,
where they had dry-rotted in stagnation for a
generation-of time, and became the instrument of
building them the present handsome church, one
of the glories of the Easter Hill cluster.”

“ The Big Irishman ” writes The Bulle-
tin :
As regards the late Salmon Wilson’s son-in-law, the
Huntingdon title is safe enough, but the claim to the Irish
estates is undoubtedly weak. In his famous essay on
Warren Hastings, after whom, by the way, Huntingdon 8
brother is named. Lord Macaulay says: “His family re-
ceived from the Tudors the earldom of Huntingdon ;
which, after a long dispossession, was regained in our own
day by a series of events. scarcely paralleled in romance.’*
This referred to the present earl’s great grand-
father, who was a sailor before the mast, when
a good-natured lawyer took up his case. I have
always heard that had the lawyer in question
not died suddenly and left his affairs in confusion,
the Huntingdon family, in addition to the title, would
have gained the estates afterwards squandered by that
Juggins of the last generation—the Marquis of Hastings.
The present earl’s grandfather married an Irish heiress
named Power. He had not a cent, and her property was
ruined in paying his pre-marital debts, and in keeping
his still poorer relations. Those who think that Ireland
must thrive under the management of Englishmen,
should witness what havoc English management wrought
on one of the most prosperous corners of the island.
When that Irish heiress’s sob, the present earl’s father,
attained his majority, she being dead, her cousin, the
eldest male representative of the Power family, laid claim
to the estates, and went so far as to serve the tenants
with ejectment notices. But the action was suddenly
dropped, and it is supposed that Power, who passed his
days in London, was “squared.” Anyhow, the law of
primogeniture was on his side, and I have heard an old
Irish lawyer declare that Power’s eldest son could still
lay claim to his estates. Perhaps this is what troubled Sir
Salmon.

WOOL RESERVES.
In accordance with their usual custom in directing
attention to all matters affecting the Wool Trade,
the National Wool Company of Melbourne, re-
mark upon the practice some woolgrowers have of
placing too high reserves upon their produce.
They point out that the woolbrokers in the
best position to* gage the current market values,
and it is best to at the first time of offering,
for in most cases a re-offered wool is neglected;
in fact, there is a clear understanding between the
wool-buyers that all withdrawn parcels are at the
option of the last bidder. It is only when the
market is very active and rising that owners have
a chance of securing better prices, while, if the
market is at all weak, the original bid is rarely
maintained. It should also be borne in mind that
wools show to the best advantage when first
opened out, but after the first handling by the
buyers it, to a great extent, loses its attractive
appearance.*

POLITICAL POINTS
A minimum wage of 7s, 6d. a day is to be
enforced in all Vic. collieries holding State
contracts.
Lord Brassey (at a banquet) on the Imp.
Federation League :
With regard to the league, there had oome a time when
it seemed that there was nothing more to be done, no
special effort to be made, and he had come to the con-
clusion that the Imperial Federation League should exist
no longer.
There was nothing done, and nothing more-to be
done. That is just about the size of Imperial
Federation.
Chief Justice Way is credited with writ-
ing some of the speeches which his brother-in-
law, Dr. Campbell, fires off in S.A. Leg. Council.
The sting of present Q. Parliament will be
in its tail. The big railway bribe will keep the
rest of the session busy, and possibly cleave
asunder Nelson’s compact crew.
Q. Attorney-General Byrnes, Griffith’s
white-headed “boy,” is to be opposed by the
same man whom Byrnes beat so decisively at last
election with the usual dummy independent
candidate to split the white votes. Byrnes’
tongue will most likely procure him a second
innings.
The turf is the fetish of one prominent
Labor politician—his vote can never be reckoned
on when there is a horse-race within the metro-
politan radius. The fact that a suburban race-
meeting bulked larger in his eyes than the
interests of his special constituents had serious
results the other day.
The appointment of Julius Vogel to be
financial adviser in London to the M.L. Govt, is
a foolish piece of jobbery. The billet, which
Vogel’s predecessor, Penrose Julyan, filled with
no great credit, at a screw of .£4OO a year, should
be abolished. Maoriland is now “advised” in
London by Agent-General Perceval, by the Bank
of England, the 8.N.Z., and by Vogel, and yet
Treas. Ward went ’Ome to look after the last
loan in person, and Seddon cabled more advice
after him.
Nothing shows the incurable Toryism of
the Nelson Ministry so strongly as its new
railway schemes. It has the impudence, in
the middle of 0ct.fd.895, to bring in plans for tin-
pot lines through trumpery districts, and announces
that the compensation will be very heavy. Not a
word about Betterment.
Brassey at least makes no vain clamor
about dear old Hingland’s devotion to “her
colonies,” Witness:—
He was bound also to say that if the mother country
had no other interests to consider than those of her own
island, if she had no other colonies to protect, her navy
would have to he as strong as it was to-day.
This tone will not suit the Victorian Tory party,
which has long traded upon the idea that the
beloved mother country exhausted herself to
suckle her brood.
The Vic. Govern-
ment proposes to lend
to the municipalities
.£50,000 of trust-moneys
without interest, repay-
able in 30 years, or a cen-
tury, or something like
that. The reason as-
signed is that the unex-
pected reduction of the
subsidy has run many
of them into difficulties,
money having been spent
in advance —and so on.
The Vic. Government
has lent a lot of money
to local bodies. Most
of them are in arrear
with their interest; some
have never paid any in-
terest at all; and, in
a very large proportion
of cases, the principal
seems hopeless. Some
of the most hopeless ones
of the lot are now how 1-
ing for further loans or
subsidies, and the ones
that howl loudest are in
difficulties, as a rule,
because they flatly re-
fuse to tax themselves
sufficiently to meet their
ordinary expendituie.
Turner might just as
well lend that £50,000
to the great sea-serpent.
Ex-Attorney -Gen-
era! O’Loghlen, rising
in his barristerial wrath
to denounce a Vic.
Crown prosecution of
alleged conspirators as a
scandalous instance of
“ the way in which the
strings are pulled,” is
rather an amusing sub-
ject for reflection.
O’Loghlen should be the
last man to suggest that the Crown has strings
attached to us works.
Premier Reid has, it is understood, in
view of an absolute dead-lock with the N.S.W.
Upper House on his taxation proposals, shaped
for himself a course of procedure which will give
ad interim, effect to the will of the Lower House,
leaving the battle of ascendancy to be fought out
leisurely afterwards. The he is said to
entertain has its nearest parallel in Gladstone s
management of the Army Purchase Bill.
Kingston’s drastic measure of law-reform
has paralysed S.A. sharkdom. He proposes
that no candidate for solicitorship need pass any
examination before being articled, take any degree,
or be examined in any dead language. To become
a solicitor a candidate need only pass in law of
property, contracts, wrongs, principle of common
law and equity, and law of evidence. The bill
also reduces court fees, and makes a heavy reduc-
tion in solicitors’ costs, and provides for the ad-
mission of women as attorneys. It has no chance
of passing, but it will be handy at next April
elections.
N.S.W. Parliament, if anxious to econo-
mise, should think of the numerous useless but
supposedly-ornamental personages of the Black-
rod pattern. Before women cleaners-out are
ousted or docked, some member with a hard jaw
should demand the abolition of Blackrod & Co.
And what about his eminence, the hangman ?
Will anyone contend that the Govt, man-butcher
is worth .£156 a year in these hard-up days?
Three pounds a week, wet and dry ! Why, a
hangman for all Australia shouldn’t cost that .
Let us federate in Hangmen ! Anything for a
beginning.
The shoddy question turned uj) again last
week in Yic. Assembly. The police uniforms are
made of imported English cloth, and cost about
£5 a suit. Maloney, M.L.A., solemnly tore a
sample of this cloth into rags with two fingers of
each hand, and as a cloud of dust and- fluff flew
around he asked the Government to patronise the
local article in future. Then he produced a
similar sample of the local goods, costing
about half the money, and professed to hang
on to it with all his strength, and it gave not
an inch. Still the result might have been different
if Murray Smith had hung on to that local
sample. It is quite surprising how different the
results may be when an enemy is hanging on to
the sample.
Three more Yic. Freetrade Ms.L.A.
“ chucked up ” the good eause last week. Bowser,
Craven and little Johnny Isaacs voted steadily
and consistently for reducing duties all along the
line till the tobacco item was reached, and then
they rose unanimously, like one lone gum-tree in
the middle of a great waste, and demanded Pro-
tection to the extent of nearly 300 per-cent. They
also pointed out that Protection, in this particular
case (though not in cither cases), helped the in-
dustry along, increased the supply, kept down
prices, supplied work, and did many other good
f things. They all represent tobacco-growing dis-
tricts, and every Freetrader wants Protection for
his own industry. Freetrade is always meant for
the other fellow, somehow.
Recent cables, indicating that a batch
of immigrants are on the way to New Cale-
donia, seem to prove that convict-transportation
to that quarter has actually ceased. This agrees
with the forecast of Consul Biard d’Aunet, of
Sydney, after a visit to New Caledonia a couple
of years ago. "’New Caledonia starts its free life
in these seas 56 years later than N.S. Wales, 42
years later than Tasmania, and 27 years later than
West Australia. But how will free-labor com-
pete with the assigned convict-labor on which the
John Higginsons of the place rely ? In 1879 the
Noumean Government entered into a contract
with Higginson, granting him the services of 300
convicts for 20 years at one penny per day per
man ; and several other contracts of a similar kind
are now current.
Dear Bulletin, —Compared with his
British brother, Australia’s Batman has been very
A BANKING ITEM .
“What’s up, Bertie?”
( I-eh-sent a proposal of marriage to Miss Jones by post, and
yesterday she returned it marked N.S.F.”
successful in evading,taxation. In the old coun-
try, the “ big ” man has not only to pay income
and land-tax when liable, but is also mulct for
inhabited-house duty and establishment licenses—
i.e., 15s. per , annum for every male servant em-
ployed ; 42s. per annum for every large, and 15s.
for every small, carriage or conveyance; 42s. per
annum for armorial bearings if painted on car-
riage, 21s. only if used as a crest on notepaper.
Then there are licences to kill game. The keeper
of a large establishment, such as the Duke of
Portland, will pay from £80 to .£100 per annum.
And the death-duties are now very heavy. The
N.S.W. “big” man, much as he is inclined to
cry out, has no reason to complain.—V.
An indignant Maoriland traveller writes
to Sydney Telegraph
From a strictly British point of view, it can scarcely be
considered a free country. The pettifogging legislation
which the rulers indulge in with regard to details of minor
importance is enough to disgust any sensible man. When
I was there in February last, a friend and I called in at an
hotel one evening and asked for cigars, and were sur-
prised to be told by the landlord that he was not per-
mitted to sell cigars on Wednesday after 1 p.m. The
Early Closing Act, which has had the presumption to
seek to regulate the actions of men and women, almost
with regard to ever3 r detail of their lives from dawn till 5
p.m., has proved a most obnoxious law, and has caused
more trouble and dissatisfaction in the colony than any
similar law for many years past. Amongst other en-
croachments oif liberty, commercial offices must close at 5
p.m. ; but, if suitable representation be made to the
local government official that it is impossible to finish the
day’s invoices by 5 p.m., a “dispensation” maybe granted
to Keep the office open until 5.30 p.m. for the purpose of
invoicing only.
THE DANGER'S OVATION.
1 .—Miss Foot Flinger receives a handsome
square floral design after her famous dance.
2.—Rushing behind the scenes she maws it
a diamond shape, and —
3.—has it passed over the footlights again ,
amid thundering applause.
4. — Then, having been dexterously t wisted.
it comes over the Jootlinhts again.
5.—One more twist makes it round,
and —
6. —the next day, critics speak of “ the
five magnificent floral tributes.”

The Royal Bank of Australia
LIMITED.
Head Office—MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
FIFTEENTH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 30th October, 1895.
On referring to the annexed balance-sheet and statement of accounts to 30th September last (duly
audited and certified), it will be seen that the bank’s business has been well maintained—the profit
earned being about the same as last half-year. The Companies Act Amendment Bill, alluded to in
previous reports, authorising the reduction of capital by writing off losses, is now before Parliament,
and no doubt it will shortly be dealt with ; in the meantime, the directors propose to carry forward, as
before, the balance at credit of Profit and Loss Account, amounting to <£17,378 ss. 6d., inclusive of
<£15,339 13s. l«*d. brought from last half-ysar. During the period covered by the accounts, financial
affairs have been somewhat unsettled (one local bank having gone into liquidation) and the directors
have considered it judicious to maintain very large cash reserves—the amount at the balance being
thirty-seven per cent, of the bank’s total liabilities to the public; this, of course, prejudicially
affects profits, but it will doubtless meet with the approval of shareholders as being in the best in-
i crests of the bank.
Since the last half-yearly meeting, Mr. David Death resigned his seat at the board, which it is
nst proposed to fill, as four directors are found to be sufficient for the effective control of the bank’s
business. A resolution reducing the number of di ectors to four will be proposed at the meeting.
By order of the board.
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

There were no moonlight rambles, garden-
gate chats, or any other sentimental nonsense
about Bullooky Tom’s courtship. While in search
one day of stray members of his team, Tom
espied Liz., the daughter of a neighboring farmer,
paddling about the brink of a pond with her boots
off. “ Good day, Liz.” “ ’Elio, Tom.” “What
yer doing there ? ” asked Tom. “Ketchin’ leeches
ter put on father’s leg,” replied the girl. “ How’d
yer like to be married ?” queried the big bullock-
puncher. “Wouldn’t mind if I got the chance,”
answered the agricultural maiden. “ How’d yer
fancy me tor a cove ?” further questioned Tom.
“You’d do,” assented the girl. “Right,” con-
cluded the wooer, “ I’ll see the old man and fix
it up.” A week later Tom and Liz. ran in double
harness.

Great Britain has compelled the Chow
Emperor, by threats of bombardment, to degrade
the Governor of Szechuan for complicity in the
recent missionary massacres, and to declare him
incapable of again holding office. It now tran-
spires that the same thing happened to the same
Governor in connection with another missionary
outbreak a year ago, and apparently he was none
the worse for it. Probably he will be none the
worse this time either.

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DU ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or

‘THE LAST OFSIX, 1
Henry Lawson’s book, which may he
called “Block and Baokblocks: Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss. 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.
There is a nice nest of squatting J’sP.
round about Muswellbrook and Scone, N.S.W.
A selector has about as much chance before them,
where squatting sympathies are concerned, as a
too-early Christian had with an arena-full of lions.
Dudley le Souef, of Melb. Zoo, loves to
tell yarns of his snakes. His Natal rock-snake
one day swallowed its blanket, and then put
away a'rabbit as dessert. For three weeks its
liver was sluggish. Then snakey got restless,
coughed up the blanket undigested, and, after
washing, both blanket and snake were as good as
new. _ A fortnight later this same serpent tried to
wrap its blanket round its inside again, but was
prevented when about half-way through. But the
big Indian python went one better, for he swal-
lowed his blanket and didn’t cough it up. The
tiger-snakes also have bad habits in this line.
Very often two of different sizes will fasten on
opposite ends of a mouse; in that case the smaller
one follows the mouse into the bigger one’s in-
terior and is never seen again. Another serpent,
Hamadryas , is a confirmed cannibal, eating nothing
but other snakes.
About three-fourths of the medicine con-
sumed by the Chinese in Australia —and they are
great drug-takers—is of European manufacture.
The more conservative Chows, however, stick
religiously to their native drugs, the manufacture
of one firm of pig-tailed apothecaries in Canton
having the greatest vogue. This business has
been in the hands of one family for over GOO years,
and is doubtless the oldest-established drug con-
cern in the world. About 40 different kinds of
medicine are manufactured, and as much as 4s. is
paid for a single pill. The trade-mark of the
firm is a severed hand, and the legend is that one
of the founders, having refused a customer
“ cledit,” had his hand chopped off by the enraged
client, whose wife had died, presumably through
the inability of the husband to procure the
precious drug. The severed hand, surmounted by
some hieroglyphics which are the Chinese equiva-
lent for ‘‘no tick,” adorns the gorgeous advt.-
cards of the establishment.

Mrs. Andrews, a charming champion
of her sex, took the stage at Melb. Athenseum
Hall, the other evening, and discoursed eloquently,
in a tasteful costume and sweet, soothing tones,
upon the matter of New Woman’s revolt against
tyrant Man. Mrs. Andrews’ lecture, which was
erudite and to the point, although it went only to
the polished side of the point, asserted woman’s
right to control the tyrant aforesaid with the
influence of her superior nature. This right, says
the pretty little enthusiast, is being more and
more conceded to the sex. “A good, womanly
love is the only power they ever had, or will ever
have, in the world. In the future, as in the past,
the real strength will be with the Womanly
Woman.” The statement is quite unoriginal, yet
it seems very fresh and fragrant these times. Also
it sounds unquestionably true from the mouth of
the lecturess. Such intellectual angels as she find
nothing in the social system to revolt against, pro-
vided that they have not been unfortunate in
their matrimonial choice- And if they do happen
to marry stingy, intractable brutes, the fault, says
this smiling matron, is their own in not having
exercised better judgment. The question as to
whether Beaut}', having committed an error of
judgment, may be entitled to bolt from the Beast,
doesn’t vex the philosophy of Mrs. T. R.
Andrews. There are men in the world whom
even she couldn’t lead with a glossy thread
plucked from her black back hair —but no matter.
Mrs. Andrews is exceedingly nice and improving,
as a woman and a lecturesss, and The Bulletin
looks forward with soulful eyes to the time when
every daughter of Eve will bring her sort of in-
fluence to bear upon sjciety. Meanwhile, poverty
is rife, and millions of women get their superior
qualities spoiled by sleeping in damp cellars and
sitting on doorsteps to dry themselves.
AN IMPENDING CRISIS.
Messrs. Frossard and Co. hear from reliable
authority that the demand for their Cavour Cigars
has of late increased so much that, the capacity of
the factory in Switzerland has been taxed beyond
its limits, and the sad fact stares in the face of
lovers of Cavour Cigars that for a short time at
least the market supply will not cope with the
full demand. The factory is taking active steps
to make the output equal to the heavy require-
ments addressed to it from all parts of the world.
It seems that it is the mildness and refreshing
aroma of these Cavours which rendered them so
quickly popular. Besides, the medical profession
is recommending them in all cases where the
nerves or the heart are inclined to be affected by
a stronger smoke. Another factor in favor of
this weed is its low price, which is a result of the
cheap shape adopted. Though the cigar is all
made by hand, the Swiss workmen roll these
cigars very quickly, as it enters into their
speciality, and the manufacturers have been
enabled to put all the value in the leaf employed,
instead of wasting the cost of production by pre-
senting to the public a cigar with a gracefully-
finished point that takes a lot of time and money
to make, but is obtained at the expense of the
quality of the leaf worked up.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
To-day (Thursday) the St. Alban’s Distribution
list closes, and the new owners of Trenton, Wallace,
Havoc, &c., will be discovered forthwith. Tim Bulletin
humbly prays that none of W. R. Wilson’s horses in train-
ing or promising yearlings may fall into the jewelled
hands of Ikey Mo. As for the St. Alban’s property,
ironical fate, prompted by Satan, is almost certain to
bestow it upon some honest radical, whose distress at
finding himself a bloated landowner will turn Iris brain,
in which case the “lucky” victim may perhaps swallow
the winning ticket and seek a watery grave. The St.
Alban’s Distribution is full of tragic and comic possi-
bilities.
The moral of this year’s Caulfie'd Cup is the
same as usual. Backing favorites for a mile-and-a-half
race round a course which offers many opportunities for
good or bad luck to operate, when 30 or 40 horses are
running to win (instead of being mostly engaged in stop-
ping behind one another), is a foolish practice, inasmuch
as there are many outsiders to choose from, and it is hard
to find a reason for fancying one of them more than a
dozen others. Where were Waterfalls pretensions to
special favor among, say, twenty-six Caulfield Cup
chances leaving eight starters, such as Miraculeux,
Brilliant, &0., out of consideration ? The colt’s
recent running was negative evidence against his ability
to win (with 7st. 31b. up) a race in which three-year-olds
h id become notorious for knocking under to older horses.
On form, as well as personal appearance, Ayrshire (7st.
41b.) was a better colt than Waterfall, yet he oouldn t stay
to fight out the Caulfield Stakes with Atlas, llova and
Havoc. Ayrshire fell in the Cup race. He had had luck.
Waterfall was one of the few that had good luck in get-
ting an opening to emerge from the mob. A lot of them
ran hopelessly bunched together all the way round. All
the same, Waterfall proved films If a smart animal by
fairly and squarely defeating Hopscotch, Trenchant,
Preston, Atlas, Chesterman, Havoc, and two or three
others that figured clear of the ruck half-a-mile from
home. He may be equal to winning the V.R.C. Derby.
But it must be remembered that Te Whiti, who made a
brave show' against Waterfall last Saturday, bad pre-
viously been “smothered ” by Wallace in the Guineas.
» • » *
All the “Gaiety ” cn., from speculative Deoima
Moore downwards, backed Waterfall at the instigation of
Norman Menzies. It was their “likely outsider” and
eleventh-hour tip.
* * • ♦
On the Caulfield Cup running, Trenchant,
Quiver, Monastery, and Chesterman have chances for the
Melbourne Cup, hut it is more than possible that some of
Saturday's “ ruck ” horses will beat this quartette over
tlie longer and better course.
The awful state of turf morality at Melbourne
suburban meetings, including Caulfield, is shown by
newspaper selections for petty handicaps. Horses are
tipped with the most cynical indifference to their recent
“ form,” which probably wouldn’t justify a prophet in
tipping them to win a donkey-race. The 11 great ’ jour-
nals encourage crock running with these calm sugges-
tions that certain animals couldn’t have been “on the
job" last lime they started.
• • * *
Victorian handicap horses were put bo com-
pletely in tiie shade at Caulfield that they seem bound 10
get all the best of it at Flemington. Otherwise hackers
will not be astonished, and a meeting deficient in astonish-
ment for backers is almost out of the question.
» • » ♦
The folly of discussing every horse’s chance in
a big handicap is very apparent in sporting sermons re
the Caulfield Cup. For instance, the Australasian man
said; “Mr. Oxenhfin's horses—Solatium and Waterfall-
have incurred penalties, but I should not fancy them if
they hadn’t." The more nags a prophet disposes of in
this way the greater Ids risk of being proved a blind seer.
* ♦ * *
The scratching of Hova for the Caulfield Cup
was a more outrageous business, if possible, than the
Ruenalf affair of last year. The cases stand on all fours.
Ruenalf was Kept in the race until the drawing of Taft’s
sweep, and when the ticketholder would not come to
“terms,” Ruenalf was scratched. Mr. Abey Davies and
his colleagues did likewise, with a like result, according
to the story told by the newspapers, and told, moreover,
as though this claim to fatten on a ticket-holder’s good
fortune were peifectly decent and just. The assumption
that Messrs. Davies & Co. did intend to run Hova if they
could get hold of the “sweep money” they wanted—
i.e., stand to win their stipulated portion was
no excuse for scratching him on the day of the
race, although it defends them from the charge
of deliberately dropping the public into the soup.
Mr. Davies himself declares that lie made up his mind to
scratch several days before he did it. For weeks past
Hova was said to be “ not. going ” tor Caulfield, but going
for Melbourne Cup, in which race he will probably prove
his owners to have been chasing a shadow. Men who
scratch first-class horses for valuable events when they
are in great fettle are generally not as “clever ’’ as they
think themselves. Even if Hova’s people mean
real business for the two-mile handicap, as is no
doubt the case, their brilliant champion, who has
invariably run like a non-stayer, isn’t worth backing
at a rattier short price, or a ralher long pi ice for
t 1 >.at matter. Besides, they have 1 id themselves open to
the suspicion of more scratching tactics to come.
Punters had heller leave Hova alone for the Cup, and
content themselves with the gladsome recollection that
Atlas jostled him out of the Caulfield Slakes when the
stable money was on.
•' • • •
M>lb. Argus was very soft and gentle in its
references to the Hova scratching : “ After all the public
is the main support of the turf—without its aid racing
would be impossible—and therefore it is entitled to a little
consideration from horse-owners." “Eren from high-
class Bass'ii-ty personages”—the Tory organ might have
added. Though Mr. Grice admits to having a financial
interest in Hova, but says he has nothing whatever to do
with its management.
» • • •
Mr. Abe Davies has unwisely furnished a
reason for scratching Hova. It was due to the faoi that
he (Abe) regards his luck as “ phenomenally had at Caul-
field ” —simply this, and nothing more. He had intended
to scratch Hova when final acceptances were due, but
decided at the last moment to let him remain in"
although surely there was no chance for bis ‘•pheno-
menal ” Caulfield luck to change between the Monday
and Saturday mornings. The notion of Abe hugging his
superstition, and more or less meditating whether he
should scratch Hova, whilst all the time the public con-
fidingly dropp d their money into Ikey Mo’s bag, is a
pleasant tiling to dwell upon. It ought now to be abun-
dantly plain in Australia tHat the punter who gambles
over other men’s horses does so purely at his own risk.
♦ * ♦ ♦

• * ♦ •
Mr, Anderson, of the V.A.T.C., holds tight to
his determination not to “pull” J. C. Bowden for libel.
“A court of honor” is the tribunal that Mr. A. believes
in, his notion of a satisfactory court being the precious
V.A.T.C. committee—composed, by the way, of his
friends and colleagues. All the while Bowden is assert-
ing that lie can back up his charge with evidence. Ander-
son’s objection to go to law looks somewhat thin. He has
merely to take proceedings, swear a denial of the asper-
sions on His honor, leave his counsel to expose the alleged
falsity of Bowden and co.’s statements, and enjoy sub-
stantial damages. The other man stands to be shot at,
but his adversary refuses to fire. Evidently a Christian
sport.
♦ » * •
“J. B. Clark’s” allegation against Arthur
Anderson, ex-chairman of the V.A.T.C., comes rather too
long after the event. Four years ago, says J.8.C., he was
shocked to find Anderson adjudicating upon a protest at
Moonee Valley when he (the aforesaid A.) had a £5O bet at
stake. What does the racing world care about such
ancient history, true or otherwise, coming from one who,
on his own showing, didn’t object to nursing the scandal
under his singlet ? Let us come to the Tim Swiveller case,
which is only two years old, or the Atlas-Hot a decision of
only a fortnight or so since. Can anyone explain why the
Caulfield stewards spent hours in arriving at decisions
upon each of those simple points, instead of getting there
in a few minutes ?
Racing stewards, by the way, are always more
likely than not to be interested parties when they
deal with a protest. If they were not “sports” they
couldn’t be stewards, and, being sports, they are
betting men, with perhaps their own “little bits,” or
those of particular friends (or enemies) depending upon
the verdict of the court. A hand of keen-sighted racing
experts, who never had a shilling on a horse themselves,
and knew nothing of other people’s transactions; who
stood quite apart from stable interests, and cared for
nobody, would be the safest stewards imaginable—but
yer cai ii’t get ’em. The best stewards available are ex-
perts of good means and sense of honor, who gamble very
lightly, and possess enough moral courage to do the
right thing every time, even though their sympathies
might sometimes tempt them to do the wrong thing
when there was no fear of provoking an outcry.
Expert control of racing, cricket, football, or
any oilier sport that lives upon public patronage, is the
only possible system of government under which the
sport can flourish. The one thing necessary is tosebct
the best experts, and the selection lies with club mem-
bers. They exercise their franchise with quite as much
wisdom as the general public show in the making of a
Parliament.

The V.R.C. office went into mourning last week
for Walter Miles, son of the chief clerk, who bad had
“the boy” working beside him for nigh upon twenty
years. Miles, junr., a good fellow and apparently as
strong as the boss, was yet another victim to innUenza-
CMm-pneumonia.
• * • •
From the sight-seer’s point of view a day s
racing at Caulfield is an absurdly expensive entertain-
ment compared with the show offered at Plemington for
the same money. Whilst the V.R.C. price for a grand-
stand ticket is 10s., the V.A.T.C. management, which
doesn’t provide good sealing accommodation for more
than two or three thousand people, may be said to charge
60 per cent, too much for a Caulfield Cup spectacle.
Also, the bird-cage tickets at 2s. 6d. are worse value
than the Fleminglon ditto at 6s. It was a paltry slip of
a bird-cage to begin with, and the people who pay their
half-crow ns for admission find a slice of the space railed
for “jockeys, trainers, and owners”—which really means
that they (the half-crowners) are expected to stop outside
the rails whilst the committee and its gilt-edged friends
go inside to see the horses saddled up.

St. Alban’s sweepists seem to be suffering from

taken in the St. Alhan’s art-uniun had drawn the race-
horse Wallace. I was present at Fleminglon and saw
Wallace win the Derby and Cup. It seemed quite clear
to me that my ticket was the winning number. The
number is 66,465.” This evidently sanguine dreamer is
put into the shade by a cocksure labouring man, who
wrote to Archie Yuille, of Melbourne, from Port Darwin.
The son of toil said :—“Dear Archie Yuille, I don't know
you a d have never met you. I have taken a ticket in
the St. A’ban’s distribution and feel that I shall win
Wallace. This is to authorise you to race the horse on
my account as you may think pioper, and remit the
winnings to yours truly, .” The Port Darwin person
didn’t slate the number of his ticket.

The first three horses in the Cup hailed from
N.S. W., as well as Te Whiti, who ran fifth. As Monastery
(4th) is claimed by S.A., Vic. candidates were out of it.
* ♦ * •
Waterfall is the second three-year-old to land
the stake (Little Jack won in ’B2). Two other youngsters
contested Saturday’s race ; Te Whiti ran fifth and Ayr-
shire fell.
* * * •
Waterfall’s capture of Caulfield Cup for
Leviathan Oxenham was full of merit, inasmuch as he
and his stable mate, Solanum, were last the first lime past
the stand. The three-year-old made up a lot of lost
ground gradually, and when he got up with the tiring
leaders it was ail over. The fact of being at a graveyard
price the night previous show ed how the public stood off
him. Even his owner did not have a very big win, and
the hooks practically enjoyed a skinner through the suc-
cess of their brother peuciller’s horse.
The fast time (2.36 f) was caused through the
pace displayed by Hopscotch in the early stages, and
ranks as second on the record, G’naroo’s 2miu. 36secs.
being as yet unbeaten.
* * • *
The Chipping Norton stable evidently had a
great opinion of Hopscotch’s pacing powers when, despite
his 141 b. penally, they instructed Elteridge to win Caul-
field Cup from end to end. The pace was such a scorcher,
too, that failure at the distance gave no cause for wonder.
Had lie lasted out a pinching drive home the teat would
have ranked as one of Carbine-like brilliancy. The de-
feat of Long’s champion lends color to the suspicion
that lie cannot stay out a fast-run long-distance race.

Trotting—a sport which The Bulletin has ever
tried to fosier—has taken on once again at Lilliebridge, :
where the Messrs. Spencer are giving a weekly night-trot. |
The presence of such fast cattle as Edith, Coolgardie,
Cleveland, Jack, Daniel Dennis, and others, makes the
winner pretty hard to pick. Two trotting riders were i
“ sent up” for life the other day, which looks as if the j
proprietors mean to keep the sport “ correct.”
Mr. “Johnny’ Deeble promises to open the |
Lady Robinson’s Beach track next month, when a trot
will be one of the events of the Monday afternoon meet-
ings. The track is a half-mile, and if the event “gees” it
will probably draw back from M.L. some of the Sydney I
trotters which are not doing over well across the water .
just now.
And if owners behave with anything like
decency it is not altogether off the cards that the pro-
prietary clubs may even once more try a trot at their
pony meetings.

Zimmerman’s Australian programme. On Oct.
28 he leaves lor Brisbane ; back to Melb. for Austral
meeting; Tasmania in Dec.; Coolgardie, Xmas ; then
S. Australia ; then right through Australia and M.L.
about March ; thence to America.
« » * *
Zimmerman on training summarized ;
1. Abstain from alcohol and smoking. Alcohol heats
the blood ; smoking depresses tiie heart.
2. Begin training, on 'the road, say, from 20 to 30 miles
a day. Take it easy at first, gradually getting into fast
work.
8. Training by walking and running exercise is not
necessary to the cyclist. Train on your machine.
4. Never train before breakfast. An engine cannot
work without fuel.
5. Hand-rubbing—or massage—after exercise is the
best method of benefiting the muscles and working off
flesh.
6. As to diet—avoid potatoes, and the less you drink
the better.
7. Saltw-ater bathing is the best, using a sponge. Few
benefit by the cold-water bath.
8. Rest when the season is over.
Zimmerman never crosses his machine in winter.

Zimmerman’s manager talked about holding a
carnival “on his own” until the Melb. Bicycle Club and
Sydney League got their backs up. Then the manager
backed down ; there was no other course—so strong are
these representative bodies. Even now Mr. Walford
will have some trouble with the M.B.C. He wants the
club to pay Zim. £l2O to race and the club can’t see it.

Megs on’s alleged time, 2-11 3-6 sec., for the mile,
at Adelaide, is most questionable. Not one of the riders
engaged thought it as fast as even 20seo. longer, and
when Megson was told of the official time he remarked
that he wasn’t having any. It was a slow-run race against
the wind.
* * * •
Zimmerman himself says that the Australian
climate quite suits him, and that he is so well now that he
gains weight in spite of training. But, after the scienti-
fically-dished tracks he has been used to, he expects that
the Australian flat-grasi tracks will much hamper him.
• • * *
By the way, we want not too much Zimmer-
man, but just Zimmerman enough.
* ♦ * *
“ Loyal Australian,” in the Argus, objects to
sending a ’96 Australian team to England—the poorness
Of available talent, he says, ensures failure, financially
and otherwise; he suggests waiting till ’9B. The
same objection could have been urged against
any Australian team whatever, for, with the excep-
tion of the ’B2 eleven, England always had indi-
vidually better men than Australia even the 82
eleven was only better in bowler Spot- and wicket-keeper
Blackham. Moreover, when one Australian eleven played
so badly that the English got tired of recording its
defeats, that eleven netted over £4OO per man. Since
Spofforth’s star set and Turner grew stale, Australia has
been praying for a mighty bowler, and it seems time that
the clergy put in their spare days in imploring for this
long-fell want.
* ♦ ♦ *
Two old cricketing identities were visible in
Bourke-st., Melb., the otherday—Jack Conway and Frank
Allan. Jack looked the Boniface and fit to strip again,
but ‘ the bowler of a century” weathers it badly and
looks like a temperance lecturer.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Grace’s average, 52.73 for 44 completed innings
is far ahead of McLaren’s, 58.1 for 20 completed innings:
MoLaren’s big score of 424 would.alone give him an average
of 21 for 20 innings. Murdoch (with 25) comes 42nd on
the English batting-list.
* * * *
Though Placke, defeated in the giants’ fight,
Progresses towards recovery, his comprehension is not
yet “all there.” He remembers little of the great
disturbance ; he can recall the first blow he got on the
jaw in the first round, and the uppercut on the jaw in the
fourth round ; and he remembers smashing Barker over
the eye. Throughout the fight, Placke felt no pain nor
did he grow weak or feel down at all. He doesn’t re-
member walking out of the ring to the dressing-room.
He has suffered no pain since being in the hospital, and
when visitors have bored him with talk he has found it
easy and convenient to drop off into a sound and refresh-
ing sleep.

• * * *
This is a true story of one of the big prizes in
the Sydney Eight-Hour Art Union. A man handed his
wife a shilling and told her casually that she might as
well take a ticket. She put the coin away and presently
forgot all about it. At last, a day or two before the
drawing, she had been out shopping, and with a load of
small parcels she got into the wrong tram for home.
While she was being whirled away in the wrong direction
a soiled youth climbed on board, nearly breaking his neck
in the process, and offered tickets for sale. She felt for
her purse but it stuck fast in her pocket, and before she
could get it out a fat man opposite bought the offered
ticket. Then she spilt the contents of the purse on the
floor, and while she was picking them up an angular
woman took the next ticket. When she had finally
gathered up her money and settled her back hair which
had come adrift, she bought the last ticket which the
soiled youth had left, and won a big prize with it. And
now she wants to know where she would have been it she
hadn’t got into the wrong car, or if the fat man and the
lean woman hadn’t intervened, or if she hadn’t forgotten
the matter in the first place, or if a lot of other things
hadn’t happened.

“Titles in these democratic days are
foolish and absurd, pitiful in the personality of
those who own them by inheritance, grotesque in
the personality of those on whom they have been
conferred.”—George Moore.
Mr. Thomas Collins, who died last week
at the age of 72, will be missed from his little
corner in the Works Office, Sydney, where he
held the position of chief messenger. He had
been in the service for about forty years, and was
perhaps better known by politicians, billet-
hunters, deputationists, and such folk than even
the Minister himself. Collins was a born diplo-
matist, and could be trusted to adroitly “pump”
and gracefully shunt even the most distinguished
of bores. He occupied a truly confidential posi-
tion, and was one of the most popular and widely-
known identities of the N.S.W. civil service.
Dear Bulletin, —In reply to Mr. Charles
Hedley’s query as to the Northern limit of the
platypus: I know of one instance where the
platypus has been found within the tropics. Quite
recently a sou of Mr. John M'Pherson, of Rook-
wood, killed one in Mellaleuca Creek, where they
I are said to exist in numbers. Mellaleuca Creek
' runs into the Fitzroy about 20 miles from where
the platypus was killed. There are no alligators,
so far as I am aware, in the creek, though they are
fairly plentiful in the Fitzroy. The locality I
refer to is about due west of Rockhampton, and a
few miles north of the Tropic of Capricorn. I
have heard of the platypus being found much
further north than Mellaleuca Creek.—J.T.S.B.,
Rockhampton, Oct. 14, ’95.

A woman has been discovered in Southern
N.S.W. who, though nearer 60 years of age than
50, has a five-months-old baby of her own.
Attention is hereby drawn to the advertise-
ment of the Australian Mutual Provident Society,
appearing in another column, intimating that all
policies issued before the end of the year will share
in the division of profits to be made as at 31st
December next. The amount of cash bonus dis-
tributed among the members for the single year,
1894, was nearly half-a-million sterling, which is,
we understand, a larger amount of profit for one
year than has ever been realised or divided by
any other Life office in the British Empire. The
Society’s prospectus, containing tables of rates for
a number of new benefits, can now be obtained
on application, and intending proposers should
procure a copy and carefully consider the same,
the tables of rates for assurances under which
members participate in the profits of the Society-
being properly distinguished from those for non-
participating policies.
William Ryan, the Australian actor, remi-
nisces about Lola Montes. She was an Irish girl,
but long simulation had given her absolute com-
mand of an attractive' broken English, and her
intimacy with the King of Bavaria destroyed
whatever she had previously known of her own
language. This Irish-Bavarian-Spanish-Melican
woman arrived in Australia in ’57, in which year
Ryan supported her at the Haymarket, Bendigo,
under Hy. Coleman’s management. Lola’s star
part —she thought it so, anyhow—was as herself in
their specially-written drama—Ryan being the
King of Bavaria. Lola was tall and willowy—a
sparkling, languorous, and yet hard-eyed brunette
—and she had the temper of seven devils. She
smoked cigarettes throughout rehearsal, and
alternately bullied and petted Follande, who was
her lord and tame eat at that time. One morning
shesaid, “ What is the time, Folly, dear?” “Folly
dear ” pulled out his watch very lazily, but ere
he could open it she had torn it out of
his hand, dashed it on the stage and broken
it under her heel. Two hours later the impulsive
Lola spent .£5O on a new watch for “Folly.”
Some miners asked her to christen a new claim.
This she did, getting gloriously drunk in the
process. That night she was “paralytic,”
missed her lines, staggered down to the footlights
and remarked to the audience that the King of
Bavaria —hie—had not given her one correct cue,
and that the King of Bavaria was very—hie—
drunk. The audience applauded. Ryan came
down the stage and stated that he was quite sober,
and that Lola Montes was exceedingly drunk.
The audience again applauded and called for a
repetition, Bendigo Mac offering .£5O if the scene
were kept up for five minutes. In 12 nights at
Bendigo the receipts were .£2400, Manager Cole-
man and Montes sharing equally. Lola was so
extravagant that her balance for the fortnight
was 13s. From Bendigo she went to Ballarat and
horsewhipped a newspaper man named Senior for
making rude remarks. Next day Mrs. Senior
gave Lola “ the father and mother of a hiding.”
In another Bendigo season Lola played the name-
part in “ Asmodeus.” During the performance
an awful storm raged, and the danger was in-
creased by the quantity of standing timber around
the theatre. Lightning struck the building,
making a hole 2ft. in diameter in the iron roof.
The lightning ran along the girders and dogs
and knocked the side of the stage right out,
set fire to the paint-room and workshop and
shattered a piece of timber 14 inches in diameter.
Eight people were standing near this post, but all
escaped. An actor named Robert M'Gowan ran
across the stage, bent almost double, his fingers in
his ears. He escaped through the shattered wall,
ran to his hotel and locked himself in his room,
and, as he refused to come out again that night,
they finished the show without him. Yet Lola
Montes came cool and smiling to the footlights
and said, in her “faked” broken English, “Ladies
and gentlemen, I have played the part of the little
devil often before, but never to such realistic
thunder and lightning.” But the peril had been
too close for burlesque, and the audience hissed
her. This remarkable woman died in great
poverty in America. She deserted Follande in
Australia. He followed her by the next steamer,
but suicided by jumping off the ship in mid-ocean,

South Wales, do hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows ; Whereas I suffered severely from
a very troublesome affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that I was quite unable to
attend to my business. I tried several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas I, as
a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rassmussen’s Purely Herbal “Alfaline Vitality Pills,”

although this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since. The Alfaline Pills also
cured my wife, and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true.—
THOS. MTJDIMAN,

At time of writing, the question of the
vacant Q. Judgeship looks like an Orange v.
Green matter. Premier Nelson, son of an Orange
clergyman, is credited with wanting to appoint a
past Attorney-General (Rutledge); Attorney-
Gen. Byrnes would like to elevate Virgil Power.
Prahran (Vic.) Woman’s Franchise League
the other evening digested a lectuyette on “ Who
rules : Man or Woman ? ” The lecturer strung a
bead-roll with the names of historically-famous
females who had ruled man in the past, and
wound up by observing that, when man u'us
ruled, it was generally by women of unmoral
character. The only exception to this rule, he
genially remarked, was possibly Adam. Alter
this, the W.F.L. listened to his discourse in stony
silence.
Mr. H. Sillier, saddler and harness-
maker and importer, of 135 Sussex-stroet, Sydney,
issues a remarkably complete catalogue, winch
will be found of great value by customers m the
remote parts of Australia. It quotes prices for
every possible article in the saddlery and harness
line, and, as the catalogue is lavishly illustrated,
the bush purchaser can select what he wants with
as much tine discrimination as the buyer on the
spot. The prices, when compared with those or a
few years ago, show how utterly the bottom has
fallen out of the business ; most of the goods sell
at what, not so very long since, would have been
regarded as ruinous prices. Quotations are also
given for freight, packing, &c., so that the buyer
at Coolgardie, or Kimberley, or Port Darwin, or
down by the Great Australian Bight, can calcu-
late to a nicety the cost delivered at his own front
door.

From the scientific standpoint, the fact
that the woman Williams was enceinte when she
deliberately drowned her little boy, at Melbourne,
is sufficient ground for supposing that she was
mentally unhinged. The immense influence ot
physiological conditions is a factor in feminine
crime too little recognised. It accounts for much
female fractiousness, fickleness, general ‘con-
trariness,” and other more serious forms of in-
sanity amongst women. Nevertheless, they are
going to hang the woman Williams. And may
the Lord have mercy on their souls !

Deal’ Bulletin,—! claim the privilege of
introducing the Yankee tramp of comic-paper
celebrity to a vengeance-thirsting people. He
appeared in a shop at Newcastle, last week.
“Say, boss, give us the price of a drink?” “No.”
“ Wal, give us enough to get a feed, anyhow.”
“Go to the sergeant— he’ll give you a feed.”
“Look here, stranger, I’d give a bit to have you
in the States for ten minutes ! ” “ What would
you do?” “ Guess I’d lay you out! ” The shop-
keeper vaulted over the counter. “ Now, then ! ”
111 see you in the States,” said the tramp, as
he started off to get there first. If he keeps up
the pace he has probably arrived. —Ponte.

Dear Bulletin,-— Many interesting cere-
conn?°^ed with the death and burial of
N.S.W. aborigines have not been noticed by the
authorities quoted by Mr. J. Steele Robertson. I
can only speak with personal knowledge of the
aborigines of Burragorang and Mulgoa. I dis-
tinctly remember the death of a young black-boy
in Burragorang about 36 years ago, and shall
never forget the soul-piercing death-cry raised by
the gins, and how all the blackfellows, several of
whom were burning-off timber for my father,
threw down their tools and, taking up the wail,
ran over to the camp. The dead boy’s mother sat
on the ground and hacked herself with a tomahawk;
then she smeared her face with white pipeclay
and glued her hair together with it, and in this
ghastly state of mourning, her head covered with
terrible wounds, she remained for several weeks.
1 was not present at the funeral, but remember
that two mounds, or make-believe graves, were
raised near the real grave—one on either side —
the object in making three graves being to puzzle
the evil spirit who comes to dig up the corpse.
The aborigines of Burragorang always carved a
number of Y-shaped characters on a tree near the
grave, and if they saw these characters imitated
on a tree by a white they became angry, and
immediately obliterated them. The Burragorang
aborigines strongly objected to the presence of
white men during their burial ceremonies, and
nothing distressed them more than to sympathise
with them, or even mention the name of the
deceased. After a death they at once shifted
camp.—H. J. M‘Gooey.
Dear Bulletin, —“A Wet Day” in a
recent issue strikes a chord of my memory. I was
at Zeehan when things were humming on our
silverfields. It was nearly always raining there,
and Zeehanites (perhaps as a consequence) used to
swear more than enough. Their champion “lan-
guage men ” were at their best when they were at
their wildest —say at 11.30 p.in., when whisky and
weather had done their utmost to produce the last
possibility in the way of moisture. In Zeehan’s
one street one rainy night at this particular hour,
I spotted a man 1 had met in the morning. He
was dry when we were introduced, but he was
swearing and boasting most promisingly, so when
I saw him leaning against another man’s door,
thoroughly soaked inside and out, I considered it
worth while giving him a hearing. He was in-
veighing against the Zeehanites when I stopped
to listen. He called them by the most objection-
able names, and asked with a yell if they could
hear. Then he paused for a reply, but getting
none, continued : “I’ll walk any blanky man in
Zeehan ; I’ll swag any blanky man in Zeehan;
I 11 run any blanky man in Zeehan ; I’ll jump any
blanky man in Zeehan ; I’ll fight any blanky man
in Zeehan ; I’ll blanky well any blanky man
in Zeehan, and if Goddlemighty comes down I’ll
take a round out of him.” Three days after this I
saw him in a friend’s hut, catching devils and con-
fining them in jam-tins. He’d got ’em bad. We had
27 wet days at Zeehan in one month—that wasn’t
the record by any means. And we had our
“ language men,” yet one policeman managed the
whole field for a considerable time, and might
have continued there in a minority of one if
“refinement” had never found its way to our
West Coast. The Zeehanites boasted and drank
and swore and challenged and threatened from
morning till nearly midnight, but their one police-
man wasn’t overworked. The man who heard
overnight from threatening lips that there would
be “a fresh face in Hell in less than two minutes,”
invariably turned up for his “ doctor ” in the
morning, and evidently felt as he drank it that he
was amongst men and brothers and hadn’t an
enemy in the world. —Theta.

A Mother .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'• Get up, Bolly 1 ”
He sat sideways on the rear horse as the two
tired beasts walked slowly up through the orchard
to the stable. The lead harness was on their
backs, the swingletrees hung on the hames ; they
paced forward stolidly, the day’s work done.
He had been ploughing ; the sod, neither too
wet nor too dry, had turned a beautifully-even
furrow. For ten hours he had held the handles
and seen the earth ridge up with mechanical regu-
larity. The rising scent of the fresh soil seemed
to breathe comfort, and, as the day wore on, he
felt the subtle intoxication of the senses which
Nature in generous moods grants to her intimates.
The breeze was laden with the perfume of orange-
blossoms; the bees hummed gleefully, content with
the harvest they were reaping; and the busy wag-
tails followed his heels in the furrow. All seemed
to bid him rejoice in life and work.
So he had gone on, hour after hour, in a kind of
walking dream, watching the mould-board throw
over the long strips of turf with the neatness and
regularity of a ruler. It was one of those har-
monious days when the mind seems lulled into
ecstacy, when the blood is in the muscles and the
brain rests.
The sun was going down when the sound of
knocking on a milk-pan at the house warned him
supper was ready. He unhitched the horses, and,
seated on Tom’s broad back, rested there quietly
while the tired animals, ungnided, followed the
familiar path. Now he felt appetite come to him,
with the sudden craving and regularity given by
physical toil. His supper of corned-beef and
pumpkin would be to him as a banquet. He
hurried the horses.
As he drew near the house, he could hear the
sound of his wife’s voice in the kitchen.
“Now, Sid, you go and chop that wood this
minute; you lazy good-for-nothing ! ”
“ I shan’t.”
“How dare you; you youner villain! I will
call your father to strap you.”
The man’s face clouded as he listened. “ I had
best keep out of this,” he muttered, as, avoiding
the house, he turned off by a longer road to the
stable. But he could not escape the sound of con-
tending voices ; as he retreated, they grew louder
and louder; and before he had unharnessed and
fed the horses his wife was at the stable-door.
“Come to this boy, father, and strap him. I
can stand his impudence no longer.” The woman
spoke excitedly.
Slowly the man finished his work, slowly he
walked to the kitchen where the boy, surly and
defiant, sulked in a corner.
“ Do as your mother bids you, Sid.”
“ I won’t.”
The man reached to the wall and took down his
razor-strop, his steady hand trembling. Then,
taking by the hand the boy, who began to scream,
he raised the strap in the air.
“I told you what you’d get, my boy !” said the
mother, triumphantly.
The strap came down, the boy yelled ; but it
was the man’s body that quivered at the stroke.
Three stripes the boy had received, when the
mother rushed forward and interposed, lacing her
arms about the lad.
“ Oh, you brute, to hit a poor child like thatt
Oh, you coward! My poor darling ! Did hi s
cruel father hit him with a strap? My precious
one ! He wants to kill you ; he is not fit to be a
father. My pet! Let me see where he has hit
yon, my lovely.”
The man threw down the strap ; and, leaving
his supper uncasted, returned to the stable-loft.
Here he turned to the chaff-cutter and wearily
ground sorghum-stalks into horse-feed. Far into
the night could be heard the monotonous, tearing
sound of the revolving knives ; but the darkness
of the shed hid from view the falling chaff, and
the tears that dried slowly on the man’s set face.

“Knowledge comes, bub wisdom lingers.”
Tennyson. “Knowledge malingers and wisdom
succumbs.”— Emmanuel Abrahams.
George Nathaniel Curzon, one of Salis-
bury’s coming young men, has an American wife,
daughter of Loiter, a millionaire, who began life
as a window-cleaner in a N.Y. drapery.
In the defence of Jabez Balfour, it is to be
pleaded that Jabez hasn’t come within the British
courts’ jurisdiction, because he wasn’t properly
extradited from Argentina. It’s quite possible
the court may discover that, whilst the body of
Jabez is in the dock, his redeemed soul has been
left on the foreign shore, in which case the law
will take out a warrant for the arrest of the flabby
saint’s spiritual nature and make another appeal
to the Argentine authorities.
More Deeming Curse. A young man
travelled on a steamboat along with the hairy-
eared criminal shortly before Ids arrest, and
became somewhat intimate with him. As they
neared land, Deeming lent him his opera-glass with
which to view the scenery, and also shook hands
with him at parting. Then that young man went
ashore and broke a blood-vessel, and lay between
life and death for weeks. He revived sufficiently
to be removed to his home, but fell into a slow
decline, and, the other day, he died. He had en-
joyed good health until Deeming lent him his
opera-glass.
In Ginscin and Isis districts (Queensland) are many
Kanaka farmers and small planters legally married to
white women, who appear quite proud of the alliance,
and unashamed of their mud-colored, mongrel offspring.
Many other women have deserted white husbands to
become the concubines of the lusty niggers.
TO A KANAKA SWAIN.
I love your strong and brawny arm,
Your glossy hide of ebon ;
Your pretty little freehold farm,
Your purse, the dress, the ribbon.
I can endure your charming smell,
For you are still a man a
Lady may admire right well,
My darling Tommy Tanna.
The planter in his mood of sin
Has still an eye to cast on
A buxom, comely Island gin
To whom he fastens fast on.
And you’re a planter, though you’re black
You swear you love—how can a
White girl turn on you her back.
Dear, wealthy Tommy Tanna?
For you’re the saviour of our land,
Our industry you cherish
Were you from Queensland ever banned
The sugar-cane would perish.
I love the country—l love you,
Come to my arms, Alamia,
You’ll find me constant, leal, and true,
My charming Tommy Tanna !
Recruits for Yic. police force are being
obtained from surplus hands in the public service.
Up to date, however, only about one in ten of
the men available has been found, on medical test,
to possess sufficient stamina for police work.
N.Q. wedding orator : —“ Ladies and gen-
tlemen, —I must tell you I ham not much of a
hauditor, but will—hie—say a few words. You
see my right-hand supporter, Mrs. Blank. Well,
me and her his the boldest cohabitants in the
township ! ”
The fishing township of Hastings, Yic., is
in the throes of a bad attack of Mormonism, and
already has a fair-sized congregation of Latter-
day Saints. Hastings is a hysterical place that
changes its religion at the bidding of each wander-
ing revivalist. It began with a sort of general
nondescript paganism. A weird Presbyterian
came along one day, and Hastings became afflicted
with Calvinism. Then a wild-eyed Baptist fell
over it one dark night, and Hastings renounced
Calvin and all his works. After a Methodist
camp-meeting, in the ti-tree, Hastings went to
live in the tents of Wesley and Whitfield. Next,
a Latter-day Saint was washed up by the tide,
and, just to show it had no ill-feeling, Hastings
promptly adopted Joe Smith and Brigham Young.
Probably the next course will be one of Confucius
or Buddha, unless Bob Ingersoll happens to go
fishing in the neighborhood.
There is quite an exodus of Tasmanian
artisans to Johannesburg, and the Island
daily press raises a mournful hullabaloo about
it. The intending emigrants are duly warned
that distant fields look green, also that they
should think twice before giving up “a com-
fortable home ” in exchange for “a place where
the blacks labor for Is. fid. a day and the chances
for artisans are few and far apart,” likewise that
they should “go on the land.” To go on the land
in any of the without a safe bank
behind one and a heart as big as a post-office, is
bad enough in all conscience, remarks a Bulletin
correspondent, but to go on the land in Tasmania
is simply Hades with all the dampers off. Owing
to the peculiar facilities offered to and availed of
by the “first families” to pick the eyes out
of the country, the Crown lands of Tasmania
to-day contain less land to the acre than any
other Crown lands known of, and a man
who would tackle the task of carving a farm out
of solid basalt or carboniferous limestone orna-
mented witli wattle-scrub and prickly Moses, must
possess a stouter heart and a smaller brain than
the average Tasmanian owns, anyhow. Those who
have the good farming land keep it sacred to
sheep, while the hopeless agriculturist who doesn’t
know how to farm and doesn’t want to learn,
messes round on a miserable mortgaged freehold
with out-of-date appliances, and works like a slave
from year’s end to year’s end, in order to pay rent
for land leased from a descendant of one or other
of the first families aforementioned, to whom he
religiously touches his hat. The worst of it is that
the poor wretches don’t seem to have any burning
desire to improve things, but regard with perfect
equanimity the rejection of the Constitution Bill—
to abolish plural voting and extend the franchise
—the ditto of the Eight-hour Bill, the ditto of the
Land-tax Bill, by the Upper House, and the
loving recep ion given by the same House to a
Hobart tramway snavel, or the land-grab of the
Waratah-Zeehan Railway Co. Despite the much-
boomed activity in mining, the industry is em-
ploying very few men, and these are manacled in
approved V.D.L. fashion by absurd restrictions.
Bent’s Terror .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the daily papers and other forms of popular
fiction, one is continually meeting with the indi-
vidual who has or had a foreboding of his own
doom—the sailor, soldier, jockey, or gymnast,
who has a conviction that he is going to meet his
death on a certain spot, at a particular hour, under
stipulated conditions. In the book we find the
prognostication amply verified in the last chapter
but one; in the newspaper the death of the indi-
vidual recalls the astonishingly accurate forecast.
Possibly Curly Bent was affected by this class
of literature. Curly was boss of our shift in the
Bald Eagle, and he appeared to be perfectly
satisfied in his own mind that he was going to be
killed “one of those days ” under a certain set of
timber in the main drive, about fifty yards from
the plat. There was nothing about the timber on
the ground to suggest this unpleasant idea, and
that particular set did not differ from any other
set in the drive, but Bent clung to his conviction,
and refused to be comforted or to give it up in
spite of reason and argument.
He avoided the fatal set as much as possible, and
blanched visibly every time he passed between the
logs. He used to say that if he were carried along
the drive in the pitch dark he could find the
dreaded spot by the awful chill that struck to his
heart whenever he got near it.
One night I saw him land on the plat, dead
white, and quaking like a man in an ague fit.
“ She is goin’ to get me to-night, Tim ! ” he said
to the platman.
However, Bent continued to live, and, as he re-
fused to throw up his job, his foreboding became
a stock joke below, and the dreaded set was known
as “ Bent’s Terror.”
In the course of time the company decided to
have the trucking done by horse-power, and two
men a shift were put on to excavate a large cham-
ber for stables, the spot selected being exactly
opposite “Bent’s Terror.” The work had ad-
vanced considerably without mishap until oneafter-
noon Curly, who had just come below, was standing
in the chamber. He was surveying the work done
by the last shift, when, without a crack or a hint
of warning, about twenty tons of stuff from over
the drive and the mouth of the chamber came
down plump, with a roar that brought the white-
faced men blundering and stumbling along the
drive, wild with excitement.
The work of rescue was begun at once, and
Bent’s foreboding was recalled with awe and deep
respect. We found two men crushed and dead,
and the third blown back against the wall, jammed
in a space where he had not room to move six
inches one way or another, but living, and with-
out a scratch.
Bent died in his bed six years later, of rheuma-
tic gout

Gov. M'Gregor, of New Guinea, sings a
loud psean in praise of the Papuan policeman who
has become the right hand of the Administration,
According to M'Gregor, the Papuan bobby is ex-
tremely zealous, showing himself anxious to arrest
everybody (“making many and regretable mis-
takes ”) he can lay his hands on. If he can only
succeed in cultivating a brogue he may yet
possibly emulate the proceedings of his Australian
brethren—even to the extent of raising a “ terrace
of houses.”
