" Cruel as the Grave ."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were only newly married, too. That
Was the worst of it. A still substantial remnant
of wedding-cake was deposited by the clattering
steward each afternoon on the small corner table
of the saloon, in company with a dainty picnic-
basket—“ wedding present ” stamped unmistake-
ably on its plated teapot and monogram-adorned
leather strap.
The sting lay in the contrast of present neglect
to the little triumphs of maidenhood. I have seen
quite a crowd of satellites round this girl as her
young beauty shone out in a ball-room. I have
heard the boys chattering about her at the club
and remarked their efforts to become introduced.
They soon fell away from their allegiance, but
there were plenty more to fill their places. Her
beauty was of a kind that attracts strongly but
cannot retain admiration,
“She palls,” I heard one young fellow explain
briefly, after a week’s devotion.
This she did not know until she married, and
she married a man peculiarly prone to satiety.
He could not speak to a woman without his over
powering weakness showing itself in the large
dilating pupils of his dark, feverish eyes. The
other woman saw that look when first she smiled
at him, but she smiled none the less for it.
His wife was certainly in love, but it was the
passion that had possessed him which she loved
and not the man himself, and what she loved had
burnt itself out during the honeymoon. All
she lived for was to see it kindle again, but she
could strike no spark.
The bride took her seat opposite me at meal-
times, but I doubt if she so much as noticed my
existence. She had no ears or eyes save for her
husband. She heard all he said and she followed
his eyes as they travelled restlessly among our
lady passengers until they met one other pair.
The other woman sat' at the end of the table near
the door, and his eyes and hers met and spoke at
every meal.
• * «
There came an evening when half-way
through dinner she looked him full in the face,
rose and slipped out. I don’t know if the wife
saw it. The husband pushed away his plate and
put his hand to his head. “ I have a bad head-
ache,” he said. “I shall go into the air for a
second. “ No,” he added sharply as she started
to follow him. “ Stay where you are. . I hate a
fuss.”
She sat down again without a word, but she
sent away each course untasted. He did not
return.
From that day a new intensity of longing filled
her face and quivered on her lips. It became an
invariable custom for the husband to leave her
with a flimsy apology to the solitary completion of
her dinner. . Once she rose and accompanied him
in spite of his impatient “Can’t you let me be
alone for five minutes?”
“ I have a headache, too,” she said.
“Then you had better go to your cabin and I
will send the stewardess to you.’’
She followed him from the saloon, but when I
went on deck I caught a glimpse of her white face
as she passed me, walking rapidly up and down
the deck, alone. After that she sat through
dinner.
• # •
One night she came into the saloon with
a face like death. Her husband was not with her,
and further down the table was another vacant
place. Her eyes were fixed and dazed as she
helped herself to each dish in turn, but never
raised a fork to her lips. I loaned across and
spoke to her—some light, jesting remark to turn
away the attention of curious passengers. I
laughed at my own sally, but my solitary laugh
struck jarringly on my ears. She shuddered
slightly, but made no further sign of having heard.
She wore a light shawl over her shoulders, and
her full white neck gleamed through it. Her
fingers, where the rings flashed, twitched nervously
at the white wool. With a sudden start she drew
it closer round her and rose to her feet. I watched
her out of the room, turned with some remark to
my neighbor, and presently I arose too and res ched
the door. There was the sound of soft laughter
and two voices :
“ I am ashamed to go in ; we are so late.”
“ But we are hungry,” objected the other.
“And appetite, you think, must conquer
shame," said the woman half bitterly.
I hung back till they entered the saloon, then I
rushed up the companion. It needed no searching
to find that little white-clad figure. She seemed
to have shrunk together as she sat crouched upon
the rail, one arm round the post and both hands
clasping her breast. She loosed her arms and
smiled softly like a child as it falls asleep. I was
still yards away when she swayed gently back-
wards to meet a great wave that swept foaming
from the bows.
Bananaland
M.A.
According to a Footscray (Melb.) corres-
pondent, there is one terrace where the inhabitants
are in so pitiable a plight that householders are
driven to make soup of their rent-books !
Birmingham (Eng.) Gazette publishes a
sublime effusion from “ A Disgusted Dunedin
Emigrant,” who quotes a “rev. gentleman” as
saying that “the circumstances of Maoriland have
produced a race of unapproachable and irreclaim-
able blackguards which indeed has developed all
the baser human passions and appetites, and well-
nigh forsaken all ideas of religion and humanity.”
That parson must have lived in Scottish Dunedin,
where the canny Hielander changes his “ tanner ”
for threepenny-bits before going to the kirk.
A man down South, charged with wife-assault the
other day, plaintively informed the Bench that it was
only recently his wife had made these complaints. He
was afraid that she no longer cared for him. The tender
lute
Rev. W. M. Alexander, Melb. : “ Wealth,
even in Victoria, is not always united with holi-
ness.” True, since the boom there have been a
good many divorces.
A Thames (M.L.) woman, charged jointly
with her husband with having caused the death
of their two-year-old child through neglect, asked
the policeman, when she had heard the warrant
read, “ Are you going to take me now ?” “ Yes,”
said the officer. “ That’s a pity—it’s my washing-
day ! ” An ideal woman !
The Vic. Agent-General reports that theirn-
ports of Australian wine into Great Britain for the
first six months of ’95 amounted to 267,000ga15.,
compared with 396,000ga15. for the whole of last
year. The wines are mostly blended. Messrs.
Burgoyne and co. now sell about 350,000ga15.
annually. It is cabled that the Vio. vignerons
have scored splendidly at Bordeaux Exhibition,
gaining five gold medals and many other awards.
When is there to he an English society,
witli a pure-bred Smith for its first president, and
a Baker, or an Edmond, or some such distinctively
Saxon-named person in the vice-chair? The latest
anti-English organisation started in Great Britain
is a Clan Donnachaidt Society, the avowed object
of which is the fostering of clan sentiment. This
sort of thing threatens to go on until the ‘‘sub-
merged tenth ” will be composed solely of unfor-
tunate Englishmen, whose ancestors were elbowed
aside by co-operating brethren of various breeds.
An Englishman’s only chance to earn his daily
bread fifty years hence will be in joining a reli-
gious clique. If he doesn’t take up with the
Wesleyans or Baptists, he’ll fall a victim to the
stick-together sentiment of Scotchmen, Irishmen,
Jews, Germans, and Welshmen.

we see many
LOST THINGS
ADVERTISED
Now, if a man’s soul be lost,
LET ME ADVERTISE
what he has lost. He has
LOST a Crown ; he has
LOST a Harp ; he has
LOST a Throne ; he has
LOST a Heaven ; he has
LOST an Eternity.
When I consider how happy a soul may he, it appears
to me to be a tremendous thing for it to be lost, even
though it should gain the world; in fact, I cannot set the
world in contrast; it is as though I should measure the
Alps by a molehill.
“ What shall it profit a man if he shall gain the whole
world and lose his own soul? ’’-—Mark, viii., 36.
The trotting-stallion Young Granville will stand this
season at Rosedale, Carapook, terms £2 10s.
Examination questions for women desirous
of the franchise : Can you pour out half a cup of
tea? Can you refrain from putting milk and
sugar in it if so required? Can you tie a knot of
any description that will hold ? Gan you sharpen
a knife on a steel ? Can you throw a stone and
hit anything? Can you keep your temper, and is
it worth keeping? When looking at a horse can
you refrain from calling the animal “ she ” and
saying that it has “ a lovely tail ? ” Do you ever
attend wonderful sales of bargains at drapers’
shops and buy old stock, supposed to be slightly
damaged, at twice its proper value? (Answering
this question in the affirmative at once disqualifies
the candidate). Do you read the evidence in
divorce and other oases when it is spicy ? Did
you ever squeeze into a court of justice to watch a
man being tried for his life? Do you belong to
the gimlet society ?
My sweet, my soul, when we are wed,
And just a little older grown,
You’ll not forget the words you’ve said,
But still remain my ownest own ;
My own to fondle and caress,
Mine, mine to cherish and amuse,
And mine to kiss and cuddle —yes,
My own to jump on if I choose.
Dear love, they say these tender troths
That lovers plight are soon forgot;
They say—the unbelieving Goths !
That youthful loves will cool a lot;
But you will steadfast be and true,
And ne’er dispute, when time has flown,
My right to kick you black and blue
When I am drunk, my dear, my own.
How I Was Had.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr acquaintance with the confidence-trick was
gained in this way.
I had stowed my swag in my bunk, and was loiter-
ing about on the fo’o’sle waiting for the steamer
to get under weigh. We were leaving Melbourne
for Coolgardie, and, within half-an-hour of going
aboard, a bronzed six-footer and myself had
become mates to make our fortunes in the far
West. My new mate’s name was Charlie
M'Ardell, and he came from Eohuca. Charlie’s
sisters were going with him as far as Perth, but
something had happened to detain them. They
were to arrive from Echuca at 11 a.m., and it was
nearly mid-day; so it was a great relief to Charlie
when he found the boat would not leave until
evening.
After lunch, Charlie proposed a trip up town to
pass the time. I had ,£57 on me, which I did not
care to carry about. I mentioned this to Charlie,
and in an off-handed manner he said, ‘‘Put it in
my box under lock and key ” —which I did, keep-
ing out £4.
We sauntered round, and whilst doing Bourke-
street a third person joined us; he shook hands
with Charlie and gave me a nod. It appeared
he had charge of the gfirls from Echuca, and
had put them aboard the boat. He had paid
travelling expenses amounting to £3 65., and as
Charlie had only brought a few shillings with
him, I paid the stranger this amount. After
receiving the money he bade us a hurried farewell,
as he had to catch the train for Lilydale. Then
Charlie suddenly remembered he had some friends
in Bast Melbourne to whom he wished to say
goodbye, and left me to find my way back to the
boat by myself.
I got on board at 5 p.m. and went to my cabin.
My swag was there, but the box had disappeared,
and there was no sign of the girls. I thought the
matter over for half-an-hour, and soon saw the
trick that had been played on me.
However, making my way aft to get ashore for
a detective, I saw Charlie talking to two girls,
who proved to be his sisters. The box had been
transferred from our cabin to the girls’ cabin in
the saloon.
We had a merry trip, and before reaching our
destination the eldest sister determined to have
me for better or for worse. And that is how I
was had.

Woman’s rights in a nutshell: —
A man claims to sit in Parliament, but he does not
claim, nor has he the power to have a baby. A woman
claims to sit in Parliament and have a baby, too. This
is really asking too much.—Qeorchna Kingscotb, in "The
Twentieth Century."
